911

912 <section id="_4">

913

914 <h2>

915General Information About Childhood Astrocytomas</h2>

916

917 <div id="_section_4" class="pdq-sections">

918<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

919<h6>

920In This Section</h6>

921<ul>

922<li>

923<a href="#_289_toc">

924Anatomy

925 </a>

926</li>

927<li>

928<a href="#_291_toc">

929Clinical Features

930</a>

931</li>

932<li>

933<a href="#_294_toc">

934Diagnostic Evaluation</a>

935</li>

936<li>

937<a href="#_105_toc">

938Clinicopathological Classification of Childhood Astrocytomas and Other Tumors of Glial Origin<ul>

939<li>

940<a href="#_279_toc">

941WHO histological grade for astrocytic tumors</a>

942</li>

943<li>

944<a href="#_281_toc">

945CNS location</a>

946</li>

947<li>

948<a href="#_282_toc">

949Neurofibromatosis type 1 (NF1)</a>

950</li>

951<li>

952<a href="#_467_toc">

953Tuberous sclerosis</a>

954</li>

955</ul>

956</a>

957</li>

958<li>

959<a href="#_283_toc">

960Genomic Alterations<ul>

961<li>

962<a href="#_sm_CDR0000779371_457_toc">

963Molecular features of low-grade gliomas</a>

964</li>

965<li>

966<a href="#_sm_CDR0000779372_460_toc">

967Molecular features of high-grade gliomas</a>

968</li>

969<li>

970<a href="#_sm_CDR0000799873_1851_toc">

971Molecular features of neuronal and mixed neuronal-glial tumors</a>

972</li>

973</ul>

974</a>

975</li>

976<li>

977<a href="#_117_toc">

978Prognosis<ul>

979<li>

980<a href="#_118_toc">

981Low-grade astrocytomas</a>

982</li>

983<li>

984<a href="#_120_toc">

985High-grade astrocytomas</a>

986</li>

987</ul>

988</a>

989</li>

990</ul>

991</nav>

992<div class="pdq-hp-patient-toggle">

993<a href="/types/brain/patient/child-astrocytoma-treament-pdq">

994Go to Patient Version</a>

995</div>

996<p id="_14" tabindex="-1">

997Primary brain tumors, including astrocytomas, are a diverse group of diseases that together constitute the most common solid tumors of childhood. Brain tumors are classified according to histology and molecular features, but tumor location and extent of spread are also important factors that affect treatment and prognosis. Immunohistochemical analysis, cytogenetic and molecular genetic findings, and measures of mitotic activity are used in tumor diagnosis and classification. </p>

998<p id="_275" tabindex="-1">

999Gliomas are thought to arise from glial precursor cells that are present in the brain and spinal cord. Gliomas are named according to their presumed clinicopathological and histological subtype. Astrocytoma is the most commonly diagnosed type of glioma in children.</p>

1000<p id="_481" tabindex="-1">

1001 According to the World Health Organization (WHO) classification of brain tumors, gliomas are classified further as low-grade (grades I and II) or high-grade (grades III and IV) tumors. Children with low-grade tumors have a relatively favorable prognosis, especially when the tumors can be completely resected. Children with high-grade tumors generally have a less favorable prognosis, but this is somewhat dependent on subtype.</p>

1002<p id="_1798" tabindex="-1">

1003The PDQ childhood brain tumor treatment summaries are organized primarily according to the WHO classification of nervous system tumors.[<a href="#cit/section_1.1">

10041</a>

1005,<a href="#cit/section_1.2">

10062</a>

1007] For a full description of the classification of nervous system tumors and a link to the corresponding treatment summary for each type of brain tumor, refer to the PDQ summary on <a href="/types/brain/hp/child-brain-treatment-pdq">

1008Childhood Brain and Spinal Cord Tumors Treatment Overview</a>

1009.</p>

1010<section id="_289">

1011<h3 id="_289_toc">

1012Anatomy

1013 </h3>

1014<p id="_375" tabindex="-1">

1015Childhood astrocytomas can occur anywhere in the central nervous system (CNS) (refer to the <a href="/types/brain/hp/child-astrocytoma-treament-pdq#_355">

1016Figure</a>

1017). Refer to <a href="/types/brain/hp/child-astrocytoma-treament-pdq#_113">

1018Table 3</a>

1019 for the most common CNS location for each tumor type. <figure id="figure_355" class="image-center">

1020<a href="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/748659.jpg" target="_blank" class="article-image-enlarge">

1021Enlarge</a>

1022<img id="_355" alt="Drawing of the inside of the brain showing the supratentorial area (the upper part of the brain) and the posterior fossa/infratentorial area (the lower back part of the brain). The supratentorial area contains the cerebrum, lateral ventricle, third ventricle, choroid plexus, hypothalamus, pineal gland, pituitary gland, and optic nerve. The posterior fossa/infratentorial area contains the cerebellum, tectum, fourth ventricle, and brain stem (pons and medulla). The tentorium and spinal cord are also shown." title="Drawing of the inside of the brain showing the supratentorial area (the upper part of the brain) and the posterior fossa/infratentorial area (the lower back part of the brain). The supratentorial area contains the cerebrum, lateral ventricle, third ventricle, choroid plexus, hypothalamus, pineal gland, pituitary gland, and optic nerve. The posterior fossa/infratentorial area contains the cerebellum, tectum, fourth ventricle, and brain stem (pons and medulla). The tentorium and spinal cord are also shown." src="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/748659-750.jpg">

1023<figcaption class="caption-container">

1024Anatomy of the inside of the brain, showing the cerebrum, cerebellum, brain stem, spinal cord, optic nerve, hypothalamus, and other parts of the brain.</figcaption>

1025</figure>

1026</p>

1027</section>

1028<section id="_291">

1029<h3 id="_291_toc">

1030Clinical Features

1031</h3>

1032<p id="_292" tabindex="-1">

1033Presenting symptoms for childhood astrocytomas depend on the following:</p>

1034<div class="pdq-content-list">

1035<ul id="_394">

1036<li>

1037CNS location.</li>

1038<li>

1039Size of the tumor.</li>

1040<li>

1041Rate of tumor growth.</li>

1042<li>

1043Chronologic and developmental age of the child.</li>

1044</ul>

1045</div>

1046<p id="_293" tabindex="-1">

1047In infants and young children, low-grade astrocytomas presenting in the hypothalamus may result in diencephalic syndrome, which is manifested by failure to thrive in an emaciated, seemingly euphoric child. Such children may have little in the way of other neurologic findings, but can have macrocephaly, intermittent lethargy, and visual impairment.[<a href="#cit/section_1.3">

10483</a>

1049] </p>

1050</section>

1051<section id="_294">

1052<h3 id="_294_toc">

1053Diagnostic Evaluation</h3>

1054<p id="_295" tabindex="-1">

1055The diagnostic evaluation for astrocytoma includes magnetic resonance imaging (MRI) of the brain or spine. For brain primary tumors, spinal MRI is usually performed in conjunction with the initial brain MRI to exclude neuraxis metastases.</p>

1056<p id="_482" tabindex="-1">

1057Lumbar punctures examining the cerebrospinal fluid for circulating tumor cells are not commonly performed in children with this disease.</p>

1058</section>

1059<section id="_105">

1060<h3 id="_105_toc">

1061Clinicopathological Classification of Childhood Astrocytomas and Other Tumors of Glial Origin</h3>

1062<p id="_106" tabindex="-1">

1063The pathological classification of pediatric brain tumors is a specialized area that is evolving. Examination of the diagnostic tissue by a neuropathologist who has particular expertise in this area is strongly recommended. </p>

1064<p id="_107" tabindex="-1">

1065Tumor types are based on the putative glial cell type of origin, as follows: </p>

1066<div class="pdq-content-list">

1067<ul id="_278">

1068<li>

1069Astrocytomas (astrocytes).</li>

1070<li>

1071Oligodendroglial tumors (oligodendrocytes).</li>

1072<li>

1073Mixed gliomas (cell types of origin include oligodendrocytes, astrocytes, and ependymal cells).</li>

1074<li>

1075Mixed neuronal-glial tumors.</li>

1076</ul>

1077</div>

1078<section id="_279">

1079<h4 id="_279_toc">

1080WHO histological grade for astrocytic tumors</h4>

1081<p id="_280" tabindex="-1">

1082According to the WHO histological typing of CNS tumors, childhood astrocytomas and other tumors of glial origin are classified according to clinicopathological and histological subtype and are graded (grade I to IV).[<a href="#cit/section_1.1">

10831</a>

1084] </p>

1085<p id="_108" tabindex="-1">

1086WHO histological grades are commonly referred to as low-grade gliomas or high-grade gliomas (refer to <a href="/types/brain/hp/child-astrocytoma-treament-pdq#_109">

1087Table 1</a>

1088).</p>

1089<table id="_109" class="table-default expandable-container">

1131<p id="_466" tabindex="-1">

1132The 2016 WHO criteria began to utilize molecular data in the diagnosis of some tumors because of the accumulation of published evidence that tumor behavior is typically driven by common biological alterations (refer to <a href="/types/brain/hp/child-astrocytoma-treament-pdq#_111">

1133Table 2</a>

1134). Within glial CNS tumors, this was most evident in changes in the classification of the diffuse gliomas, which were grouped together based on genetic driver mutations rather than histopathological similarities.[<a href="#cit/section_1.2">

11352</a>

1136] Two types of diffuse gliomas are no longer considered distinct entities: fibrillary astrocytoma and protoplasmic astrocytoma. Epithelioid glioblastoma is a new, provisionally included variant that is categorized as one subtype of <em class="gene-name">

1137IDH</em>

1138&#8211;wild-type glioblastoma. </p>

11701</a>

1171,<a href="#cit/section_1.2">

11781</a>

11881</a>

11906</a>

1318</section>

1319<section id="_281">

1320<h4 id="_281_toc">

1321CNS location</h4>

1322<p id="_112" tabindex="-1">

1323Childhood astrocytomas and other tumors of glial origin can occur anywhere in the CNS, although each tumor type tends to have common CNS locations (refer to <a href="/types/brain/hp/child-astrocytoma-treament-pdq#_113">

1324Table 3</a>

1325).</p>

1326<table id="_113" class="table-default expandable-container">

1327<caption>

1328Table 3. Common Central Nervous System (CNS) Locations for Childhood Astrocytomas and Other Tumors of Glial Origin</caption>

1329<colgroup>

1330<col width="50.00%">

1331<col width="50.00%">

1332</colgroup>

1333<THead>

1334<tr>

1335<th align="Center" scope="col">

1336Tumor Type</th>

1337<th align="Center" scope="col">

1338Common CNS Location</th>

1339</tr>

1340</THead>

1341<tbody>

1342<tr>

1343<td>

1344Pilocytic astrocytoma</td>

1345<td>

1346Optic nerve, optic chiasm/hypothalamus, thalamus and basal ganglia, cerebral hemispheres, cerebellum, and brain stem; and spinal cord (rare)</td>

1347</tr>

1348<tr>

1349<td>

1350Pleomorphic xanthoastrocytoma</td>

1351<td>

1352Superficial location in cerebrum (temporal lobe preferentially)</td>

1353</tr>

1354<tr>

1355<td>

1356Diffuse astrocytoma</td>

1357<td>

1358Cerebrum (frontal and temporal lobes), brain stem, spinal cord, optic nerve, optic chiasm, optic pathway, hypothalamus, and thalamus</td>

1359</tr>

1360<tr>

1361<td>

1362Anaplastic astrocytoma, glioblastoma </td>

1363<td>

1364Cerebrum; occasionally cerebellum, brain stem, and spinal cord</td>

1365</tr>

1366<tr>

1367<td>

1368Diffuse midline glioma, H3 K27M-mutant</td>

1369<td>

1370Pons, thalamus, spinal cord, and other midline structures</td>

1371</tr>

1372</tbody>

1373</table>

1374<p id="_114" tabindex="-1">

1375<strong>

1376Cerebellum: </strong>

1377More than 80% of astrocytomas located in the cerebellum are low grade (pilocytic grade I) and often cystic; most of the remainder are diffuse grade II astrocytomas. Malignant astrocytomas in the cerebellum are rare.[<a href="#cit/section_1.1">

13781</a>

1379,<a href="#cit/section_1.2">

13802</a>

1381] The presence of certain histological features (e.g., MIB-1 rate, anaplasia) has been used retrospectively to predict event-free survival for pilocytic astrocytomas arising in the cerebellum or other locations.[<a href="#cit/section_1.7">

13827</a>

1383-<a href="#cit/section_1.9">

13849</a>

1385]</p>

1386<p id="_234" tabindex="-1">

1387<strong>

1388Brain stem: </strong>

1389Astrocytomas arising in the brain stem may be either high grade or low grade, with the frequency of either type being highly dependent on the location of the tumor within the brain stem.[<a href="#cit/section_1.10">

139010</a>

1391,<a href="#cit/section_1.11">

139211</a>

1393] Tumors not involving the pons are overwhelmingly low-grade gliomas (e.g., tectal gliomas of the midbrain), whereas tumors located exclusively in the pons without exophytic components are largely diffuse midline gliomas (e.g., diffuse intrinsic pontine gliomas with the H3 K27M-mutant genotype).[<a href="#cit/section_1.10">

139410</a>

1395,<a href="#cit/section_1.11">

139611</a>

1397] (Refer to the PDQ summary on <a href="/types/brain/hp/child-glioma-treatment-pdq">

1398Childhood Brain Stem Glioma Treatment</a>

1399 for more information.)</p>

1400<p id="_297" tabindex="-1">

1401<strong>

1402Cerebrum: </strong>

1403High-grade astrocytomas are often locally invasive and extensive and tend to occur above the tentorium in the cerebrum.[<a href="#cit/section_1.12">

140412</a>

1405] Spread via the subarachnoid space may occur. Metastasis outside of the CNS has been reported but is extremely infrequent until multiple local relapses have occurred. </p>

1406<p id="_211" tabindex="-1">

1407<strong>

1408Gliomatosis cerebri</strong>

1409 is no longer considered a distinct entity, but rather to be a growth pattern found in some diffuse astrocytic tumors and, occasionally, oligodendroglial tumors. The growth pattern encompasses widespread involvement of the cerebral hemispheres, often extending caudally to affect the brain stem, cerebellum, and/or spinal cord.[<a href="#cit/section_1.1">

14101</a>

1411] This pattern rarely arises in the cerebellum and spreads rostrally.[<a href="#cit/section_1.13">

141213</a>

1413] Patients with gliomatosis cerebri may respond to treatment initially, but overall have a poor prognosis.[<a href="#cit/section_1.14">

141414</a>

1415]</p>

1416</section>

1417<section id="_282">

1418<h4 id="_282_toc">

1419Neurofibromatosis type 1 (NF1)</h4>

1420<p id="_115" tabindex="-1">

1421Children with NF1 have an increased propensity to develop WHO grade I and grade II astrocytomas in the visual (optic) pathway; up to 20% of patients with NF1 will develop an optic pathway glioma. In these patients, the tumor may be found on screening evaluations when the child is asymptomatic or has apparent static neurologic and/or visual deficits. </p>

1422<p id="_395" tabindex="-1">

1423Pathological confirmation is frequently not obtained in asymptomatic patients; when biopsies have been performed, these tumors have been found to be predominantly pilocytic (grade I) rather than diffuse astrocytic tumors.[<a href="#cit/section_1.2">

14242</a>

1425,<a href="#cit/section_1.5">

14265</a>

1427,<a href="#cit/section_1.15">

142815</a>

1429]</p>

1430<p id="_298" tabindex="-1">

1431In general, treatment is not required for incidental tumors found with surveillance neuroimaging. Symptomatic lesions, often causing vision impairment, or those that have radiographically progressed may require treatment.[<a href="#cit/section_1.16">

143216</a>

1433]</p>

1434</section>

1435<section id="_467">

1436<h4 id="_467_toc">

1437Tuberous sclerosis</h4>

1438<p id="_468" tabindex="-1">

1439Patients with tuberous sclerosis have a predilection for low-grade glioma development, especially subependymal giant cell astrocytomas. Mutations in either <em class="gene-name">

1440TSC1</em>

1441 or <em class="gene-name">

1442TSC2</em>

1443 cause pathway alterations that impact the mammalian target of rapamycin (mTOR) pathway, leading to increases in proliferation. Subependymal giant cell astrocytomas have been sensitive to targeted approaches via inhibition of the mTOR pathway.[<a href="#cit/section_1.17">

144417</a>

1445]</p>

1446</section>

1447</section>

1448<section id="_283">

1449<h3 id="_283_toc">

1450Genomic Alterations</h3>

1451<section id="_sm_CDR0000779371_457">

1452<h4 id="_sm_CDR0000779371_457_toc">

1453Molecular features of low-grade gliomas</h4>

1454<section id="_sm_CDR0000779371_475">

1455<h5 id="_sm_CDR0000779371_475_toc">

1456Pilocytic and diffuse astrocytomas</h5>

1457<p id="_sm_CDR0000779371_210" tabindex="-1">

1458Genomic alterations involving activation of <em class="gene-name">

1459BRAF</em>

1460 and the ERK/MAPK pathway are very common in sporadic cases of pilocytic astrocytoma, a type of low-grade glioma.</p>

1461<section id="_sm_CDR0000779371_481">

1462<h6 id="_sm_CDR0000779371_481_toc">

1463<em class="gene-name">

1464BRAF</em>

1465-<em class="gene-name">

1466KIAA1549</em>

1467 alterations</h6>

1468<p id="_sm_CDR0000779371_406" tabindex="-1">

1469<em class="gene-name">

1470BRAF</em>

1471 activation in pilocytic astrocytoma occurs most commonly through a <em class="gene-name">

1472BRAF</em>

1473-<em class="gene-name">

1474KIAA1549</em>

1475 gene fusion, producing a fusion protein that lacks the BRAF regulatory domain.[<a href="#cit/section_1.18">

147618</a>

1477-<a href="#cit/section_1.22">

147822</a>

1479] This fusion is seen in most infratentorial and midline pilocytic astrocytomas, but is present at lower frequency in supratentorial (hemispheric) tumors.[<a href="#cit/section_1.18">

148018</a>

1481,<a href="#cit/section_1.19">

148219</a>

1483,<a href="#cit/section_1.23">

148423</a>

1485-<a href="#cit/section_1.28">

148628</a>

1487] </p>

1488<p id="_sm_CDR0000779371_407" tabindex="-1">

1489Presence of the <em class="gene-name">

1490BRAF-KIAA1549</em>

1491 fusion predicted a better clinical outcome (progression-free survival [PFS] and overall survival [OS]) in one report that described children with incompletely resected low-grade gliomas.[<a href="#cit/section_1.27">

149227</a>

1493] However, other factors such as <em class="gene-name">

1494CDKN2A</em>

1495 deletion, whole chromosome 7 gain, and tumor location may modify the impact of the <em class="gene-name">

1496BRAF</em>

1497 mutation on outcome.[<a href="#cit/section_1.29">

149829</a>

1499]; [<a href="#cit/section_1.30">

150030</a>

1501][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

1502Level of evidence C2</a>

1503] Progression to high-grade glioma is rare for pediatric low-grade glioma with the <em class="gene-name">

1504BRAF-KIAA1549</em>

1505 fusion.[<a href="#cit/section_1.31">

150631</a>

1507]</p>

1508<p id="_sm_CDR0000779371_408" tabindex="-1">

1509<em class="gene-name">

1510BRAF</em>

1511 activation through the <em class="gene-name">

1512BRAF-KIAA1549</em>

1513 fusion has also been described in other pediatric low-grade gliomas (e.g., pilomyxoid astrocytoma).[<a href="#cit/section_1.26">

151426</a>

1515,<a href="#cit/section_1.27">

151627</a>

1517] Other genomic alterations in pilocytic astrocytomas that can activate the ERK/MAPK pathway (e.g., alternative <em class="gene-name">

1518BRAF</em>

1519 gene fusions, <em class="gene-name">

1520RAF1</em>

1521 rearrangements, <em class="gene-name">

1522RAS</em>

1523 mutations, and <em class="gene-name">

1524BRAF</em>

1525 V600E point mutations) are less commonly observed.[<a href="#cit/section_1.19">

152619</a>

1527,<a href="#cit/section_1.21">

152821</a>

1529,<a href="#cit/section_1.22">

153022</a>

1531,<a href="#cit/section_1.32">

153232</a>

1533]</p>

1534</section>

1535<section id="_sm_CDR0000779371_482">

1536<h6 id="_sm_CDR0000779371_482_toc">

1537<em class="gene-name">

1538BRAF</em>

1539 V600E mutations</h6>

1540<p id="_sm_CDR0000779371_409" tabindex="-1">

1541<em class="gene-name">

1542BRAF</em>

1543 V600E point mutations are occasionally observed in pilocytic astrocytoma; the mutations are also observed in nonpilocytic pediatric low-grade gliomas, including ganglioglioma,[<a href="#cit/section_1.33">

154433</a>

1545] desmoplastic infantile ganglioglioma, and approximately two-thirds of pleomorphic xanthoastrocytomas.[<a href="#cit/section_1.34">

154634</a>

1547-<a href="#cit/section_1.36">

154836</a>

1549]</p>

1550<p id="_sm_CDR0000779371_485" tabindex="-1">

1551Studies have observed the following:</p>

1552<div class="pdq-content-list">

1553<ul id="_sm_CDR0000779371_465">

1554<li>

1555In a retrospective series of more than 400 children with low-grade gliomas, 17% of tumors were <em class="gene-name">

1556BRAF</em>

1557 V600E mutant. The 10-year PFS rate was 27% for <em class="gene-name">

1558BRAF</em>

1559 V600E&#8211;mutant cases, compared with 60% for cases whose tumors did not harbor that mutation. Additional factors associated with this poor prognosis included subtotal resection and <em class="gene-name">

1560CDKN2A</em>

1561 deletion.[<a href="#cit/section_1.37">

156237</a>

1563] Even in patients who underwent a gross-total resection, recurrence was noted in one-third of these cases, suggesting that <em class="gene-name">

1564BRAF</em>

1565 V600E tumors have a more invasive phenotype than do other low-grade glioma variants.</li>

1566<li>

1567In a similar analysis, children with diencephalic low-grade astrocytomas with a <em class="gene-name">

1568BRAF</em>

1569 V600E mutation had a 5-year PFS rate of 22%, compared with a PFS rate of 52% in children who were <em class="gene-name">

1570BRAF</em>

1571 wild-type.[<a href="#cit/section_1.38">

157238</a>

1573][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

1574Level of evidence C2</a>

1575]</li>

1576<li>

1577 The frequency of the <em class="gene-name">

1578BRAF </em>

1579V600E mutation was significantly higher in pediatric low-grade glioma that transformed to high-grade glioma (8 of 18 cases) than was the frequency of the mutation in cases that did not transform to high-grade glioma (10 of 167 cases).[<a href="#cit/section_1.31">

158031</a>

1581]</li>

1582</ul>

1583</div>

1584</section>

1585<section id="_sm_CDR0000779371_483">

1586<h6 id="_sm_CDR0000779371_483_toc">

1587Other mutations</h6>

1588<p id="_sm_CDR0000779371_373" tabindex="-1">

1589 Activating mutations in <em class="gene-name">

1590FGFR1</em>

1591, <em class="gene-name">

1592PTPN11</em>

1593, and <em class="gene-name">

1594NTRK2</em>

1595 fusion genes have also been identified in noncerebellar pilocytic astrocytomas.[<a href="#cit/section_1.39">

159639</a>

1597] In pediatric grade II diffuse astrocytomas, the most common alterations reported (up to 53% of tumors) are rearrangements in the MYB family of transcription factors.[<a href="#cit/section_1.40">

159840</a>

1599,<a href="#cit/section_1.41">

160041</a>

1601]</p>

1602</section>

1603</section>

1604<section id="_sm_CDR0000779371_476">

1605<h5 id="_sm_CDR0000779371_476_toc">

1606Angiocentric gliomas</h5>

1607<p id="_sm_CDR0000779371_468" tabindex="-1">

1608 Angiocentric gliomas typically arise in children and young adults as cerebral tumors presenting with seizures.[<a href="#cit/section_1.2">

16092</a>

1610] </p>

1611<p id="_sm_CDR0000779371_484" tabindex="-1">

1612Two reports in 2016 identified <em class="gene-name">

1613MYB</em>

1614 gene alterations as being present in almost all cases diagnosed as angiocentric glioma, with <em class="gene-name">

1615QKI</em>

1616 being the primary fusion partner in cases where fusion-partner testing was possible.[<a href="#cit/section_1.42">

161742</a>

1618,<a href="#cit/section_1.43">

161943</a>

1620] While angiocentric gliomas most commonly occur supratentorially, brain stem angiocentric gliomas with <em class="gene-name">

1621MYB-QKI</em>

1622 fusions have also been reported.[<a href="#cit/section_1.44">

162344</a>

1624,<a href="#cit/section_1.45">

162545</a>

1626]</p>

1627</section>

1628<section id="_sm_CDR0000779371_477">

1629<h5 id="_sm_CDR0000779371_477_toc">

1630Astroblastomas</h5>

1631<p id="_sm_CDR0000779371_469" tabindex="-1">

1632 Astroblastomas are defined histologically as glial neoplasms composed of GFAP-positive cells and contain astroblastic pseudorosettes that often demonstrate sclerosis. Astroblastomas are diagnosed primarily in childhood through young adulthood.[<a href="#cit/section_1.2">

16332</a>

1634]</p>

1635<p id="_sm_CDR0000779371_486" tabindex="-1">

1636The following studies have described genomic alterations associated with astroblastoma:</p>

1637<div class="pdq-content-list">

1638<ul id="_sm_CDR0000779371_470">

1639<li>

1640 A report describing a molecular classification of CNS primitive neuroectodermal tumors (PNETs) identified an entity termed <em>

1641CNS high-grade neuroepithelial tumor with <em class="gene-name">

1642MN1</em>

1643 alteration</em>

1644 (CNS HGNET-MN1) that was characterized by gene fusions involving <em class="gene-name">

1645MN1</em>

1646.[<a href="#cit/section_1.46">

164746</a>

1648] Most tumors with a histologic diagnosis of astroblastoma (16 of 23) belonged to this molecularly defined entity.</li>

1649<li>

1650A report of 27 histologically defined astroblastomas found that 10 cases had <em class="gene-name">

1651MN1</em>

1652 rearrangements, 7 cases had <em class="gene-name">

1653BRAF</em>

1654 rearrangements, and 2 cases had <em class="gene-name">

1655RELA</em>

1656 rearrangements.[<a href="#cit/section_1.47">

165747</a>

1658] Methylation array analysis showed that the cases with <em class="gene-name">

1659MN1</em>

1660 rearrangements clustered with CNS HGNET-MN1, the <em class="gene-name">

1661BRAF</em>

1662-mutated cases clustered with pleomorphic xanthoastrocytomas, and the <em class="gene-name">

1663RELA</em>

1664 cases clustered with ependymomas. </li>

1665<li>

1666Genomic evaluation of eight cases of astroblastoma identified four with <em class="gene-name">

1667MN1</em>

1668 alterations. Of the remaining four cases, two had genomic alterations consistent with high-grade glioma and two cases could not be classified on the basis of their molecular characteristics.[<a href="#cit/section_1.48">

166948</a>

1670]</li>

1671<li>

1672A study described eight cases of astroblastoma. All five cases that underwent fluorescence <em>

1673in situ</em>

1674 hybridization analysis showed <em class="gene-name">

1675MN1</em>

1676 rearrangements.[<a href="#cit/section_1.49">

167749</a>

1678]</li>

1679</ul>

1680</div>

1681<p id="_sm_CDR0000779371_471" tabindex="-1">

1682These reports suggest that the histologic diagnosis of astroblastoma encompasses a heterogeneous group of genomically defined entities; astroblastomas with <em class="gene-name">

1683MN1</em>

1684 fusions represent a distinctive subset of histologically diagnosed cases.[<a href="#cit/section_1.50">

168550</a>

1686] </p>

1687</section>

1688<section id="_sm_CDR0000779371_487">

1689<h5 id="_sm_CDR0000779371_487_toc">

1690Neurofibromatosis type 1 (NF1)</h5>

1691<p id="_sm_CDR0000779371_473" tabindex="-1">

1692Children with NF1-associated low-grade gliomas often have tumors in the optic pathway that are not biopsied. In a series of pediatric patients (n = 17; median age, 10 years) with NF1-associated low-grade gliomas in which tissue was collected and subjected to whole-exome sequencing, the number of mutations was very low (median, 6 per case).[<a href="#cit/section_1.51">

169351</a>

1694] Germline <em class="gene-name">

1695NF1</em>

1696 mutations were observed in 88% of patients, and the most common somatic alteration was loss of heterozygosity for <em class="gene-name">

1697NF1</em>

1698, with a smaller number of cases showing inactivating mutations in the second <em class="gene-name">

1699NF1</em>

1700 allele. <em class="gene-name">

1701CDKN2A</em>

1702 loss was observed in 1 of 17 patients (6%). Alterations in <em class="gene-name">

1703TP53</em>

1704 and <em class="gene-name">

1705ATRX</em>

1706 were not observed among the 17 pediatric patients with NF1-associated low-grade gliomas. Activating <em class="gene-name">

1707BRAF</em>

1708 genomic alterations are uncommon in pilocytic astrocytoma and other low-grade gliomas occurring in children with NF1.[<a href="#cit/section_1.25">

170925</a>

1710,<a href="#cit/section_1.51">

171151</a>

1712]</p>

1713</section>

1714<section id="_sm_CDR0000779371_479">

1715<h5 id="_sm_CDR0000779371_479_toc">

1716Tuberous sclerosis</h5>

1717<p id="_sm_CDR0000779371_474" tabindex="-1">

1718 Most children with tuberous sclerosis have a germline mutation in one of two tuberous sclerosis genes (<em class="gene-name">

1719TSC1</em>

1720 or <em class="gene-name">

1721TSC2</em>

1722). Either of these mutations results in activation of the mammalian target of rapamycin (mTOR) complex 1. These children are at risk of developing subependymal giant cell astrocytomas, cortical tubers, and subependymal nodules. Because subependymal giant cell astrocytomas are driven by mTOR activation, mTOR inhibitors are active agents that can induce tumor regression in children with these tumors.[<a href="#cit/section_1.52">

172352</a>

1724]</p>

1725</section>

1726</section>

1727<section id="_sm_CDR0000779372_460">

1728<h4 id="_sm_CDR0000779372_460_toc">

1729Molecular features of high-grade gliomas</h4>

1730<p id="_sm_CDR0000779372_271" tabindex="-1">

1731Pediatric high-grade gliomas, especially glioblastoma multiforme, are biologically distinct from those arising in adults.[<a href="#cit/section_1.53">

173253</a>

1733-<a href="#cit/section_1.56">

173456</a>

1735] </p>

1736<section id="_sm_CDR0000779372_480">

1737<h5 id="_sm_CDR0000779372_480_toc">

1738Subgroups identified using DNA methylation patterns</h5>

1739<p id="_sm_CDR0000779372_272" tabindex="-1">

1740Pediatric high-grade gliomas can be separated into distinct subgroups on the basis of epigenetic patterns (DNA methylation), and these subgroups show distinguishing chromosome copy number gains/losses and gene mutations in the tumor.[<a href="#cit/section_1.57">

174157</a>

1742-<a href="#cit/section_1.59">

174359</a>

1744] Particularly distinctive subtypes of pediatric high-grade gliomas are those with recurring mutations at specific amino acids in histone genes, and together these account for approximately one-half of pediatric high-grade gliomas.[<a href="#cit/section_1.59">

174559</a>

1746]</p>

1747<p id="_sm_CDR0000779372_486" tabindex="-1">

1748The following pediatric high-grade glioma subgroups were identified on the basis of their DNA methylation patterns, and they show distinctive molecular and clinical characteristics:[<a href="#cit/section_1.59">

174959</a>

1750]</p>

1751<div class="pdq-content-list">

1752<ol id="_sm_CDR0000779372_470">

1753<li>

1754<strong>

1755The histone K27 mutations: H3.3 (<em class="gene-name">

1756H3F3A</em>

1757) and H3.1 (<em class="gene-name">

1758HIST1H3B</em>

1759 and, rarely, <em class="gene-name">

1760HIST1H3C</em>

1761) mutations at K27:</strong>

1762 The histone K27&#8211;mutated cases occur predominantly in middle childhood (median age, approximately 10 years), are almost exclusively midline (thalamus, brain stem, and spinal cord), and carry a very poor prognosis. The 2016 WHO classification groups these cancers into a single entity&#8212;diffuse midline glioma, H3 K27M&#8211;mutant&#8212;although there are clinical and biological distinctions between cases with H3.3 and H3.1 mutations, as described below.[<a href="#cit/section_1.2">

17632</a>

1764] These cases can be diagnosed using immunohistochemistry to identify the presence of K27M.<div class="pdq-content-list">

1765<ul id="_sm_CDR0000779372_471">

1766<li>

1767<strong>

1768H3.3K27M:</strong>

1769 H3.3K27M cases occur throughout the midline and pons, account for approximately 60% of cases in these locations, and commonly present between the ages of 5 and 10 years.[<a href="#cit/section_1.59">

177059</a>

1771] The prognosis for H3.3K27M patients is especially poor, with a median survival of less than 1 year; the 2-year survival is less than 5%.[<a href="#cit/section_1.59">

177259</a>

1773] Leptomeningeal dissemination is frequently observed in H3.3K27M patients.[<a href="#cit/section_1.60">

177460</a>

1775]</li>

1776<li>

1777<strong>

1778H3.1K27M:</strong>

1779 H3.1K27M cases are approximately fivefold less common than H3.3K27M cases. They occur primarily in the pons and present at a younger age than other H3.3K27M cases (median age, 5 years vs. 6&#8211;10 years). These cases have a slightly more favorable prognosis than do H3.3K27M cases (median survival, 15 months vs. 11 months). Mutations in <em class="gene-name">

1780ACVR1</em>

1781, which is also the mutation observed in the genetic condition fibrodysplasia ossificans progressiva, are present in a high proportion of H3.1K27M cases.[<a href="#cit/section_1.59">

178259</a>

1783,<a href="#cit/section_1.61">

178461</a>

1785,<a href="#cit/section_1.62">

178662</a>

1787]</li>

1788<li>

1789<strong>

1790H3.2K27M:</strong>

1791 Rarely, K27M mutations are also identified in H3.2 (<em class="gene-name">

1792HIST2H3C</em>

1793) cases.[<a href="#cit/section_1.59">

179459</a>

1795]</li>

1796</ul>

1797</div>

1798</li>

1799<li>

1800<strong>

1801H3.3 (<em class="gene-name">

1802H3F3A</em>

1803) mutation at G34:</strong>

1804 The H3.3G34 subtype arises from H3.3 glycine 34 to arginine/valine (G34R/V) mutations.[<a href="#cit/section_1.57">

180557</a>

1806,<a href="#cit/section_1.58">

180758</a>

1808] This subtype presents in older children and young adults (median age, 14&#8211;18 years) and arises exclusively in the cerebral cortex.[<a href="#cit/section_1.57">

180957</a>

1810,<a href="#cit/section_1.58">

181158</a>

1812] H3.3G34 cases commonly have mutations in <em class="gene-name">

1813TP53</em>

1814 and <em class="gene-name">

1815ATRX</em>

1816 (95% and 84% of cases, respectively, in one large series) and show widespread hypomethylation across the whole genome. In a series of 95 patients with the H3.3G34 subtype, 44% of patients also had a mutation in <em class="gene-name">

1817PDGFRA</em>

1818 at the time of diagnosis, and 81% of patients had <em class="gene-name">

1819PDGFRA</em>

1820 mutations observed at relapse.[<a href="#cit/section_1.63">

182163</a>

1822]<p id="_sm_CDR0000779372_492" tabindex="-1">

1823Patients with <em class="gene-name">

1824H3F3A</em>

1825 mutations are at high risk of treatment failure,[<a href="#cit/section_1.64">

182664</a>

1827] but the prognosis is not as poor as that of patients with histone 3.1 or 3.3 K27M mutations.[<a href="#cit/section_1.58">

182858</a>

1829] O-6-methylguanine-DNA methyltransferase (MGMT) methylation is observed in approximately two-thirds of cases, and aside from the <em class="gene-name">

1830IDH1</em>

1831-mutated subtype (see below), the H3.3G34 subtype is the only pediatric high-grade glioma subtype that demonstrates MGMT methylation rates exceeding 20%.[<a href="#cit/section_1.59">

183259</a>

1833]</p>

1834</li>

1835<li>

1836<strong>

1837<em class="gene-name">

1838IDH1</em>

1839 mutation: </strong>

1840<em class="gene-name">

1841IDH1</em>

1842-mutated cases represent a small percentage of pediatric high-grade gliomas (approximately 5%), and pediatric high-grade glioma patients whose tumors have <em class="gene-name">

1843IDH1</em>

1844 mutations are almost exclusively older adolescents (median age in a pediatric population, 16 years) with hemispheric tumors.[<a href="#cit/section_1.59">

184559</a>

1846]<em class="gene-name">

1847 IDH1</em>

1848-mutated cases often show <em class="gene-name">

1849TP53</em>

1850 mutations, MGMT promoter methylation, and a glioma-CpG island methylator phenotype (G-CIMP).[<a href="#cit/section_1.57">

185157</a>

1852,<a href="#cit/section_1.58">

185358</a>

1854] Pediatric patients with <em class="gene-name">

1855IDH1</em>

1856 mutations show a more favorable prognosis than do other pediatric glioblastoma multiforme patients; 5-year overall survival (OS) rates exceed 60% for pediatric patients with <em class="gene-name">

1857IDH1</em>

1858 mutations, compared with 5-year OS rates of less than 20% for patients with wild-type <em class="gene-name">

1859IDH1</em>

1860.[<a href="#cit/section_1.59">

186159</a>

1862]</li>

1863<li>

1864<strong>

1865Pleomorphic xanthoastrocytoma (PXA)&#8211;like:</strong>

1866 Approximately 10% of pediatric high-grade gliomas have DNA methylation patterns that are PXA-like.[<a href="#cit/section_1.58">

186758</a>

1868] PXA-like cases commonly have <em class="gene-name">

1869BRAF</em>

1870 V600E mutations and a relatively favorable outcome (approximately 50% survival at 5 years).[<a href="#cit/section_1.59">

187159</a>

1872,<a href="#cit/section_1.64">

187364</a>

1874]</li>

1875<li>

1876<strong>

1877Low-grade glioma&#8211;like:</strong>

1878 A small subset of pediatric brain tumors with the histological appearance of high-grade gliomas show DNA methylation patterns like those of low-grade gliomas.[<a href="#cit/section_1.58">

187958</a>

1880,<a href="#cit/section_1.59">

188159</a>

1882] These cases are primarily observed in young patients (median age, 4 years); 10 of 16 infants with a glioblastoma multiforme diagnosis were in the low-grade glioma&#8211;like group.[<a href="#cit/section_1.59">

188359</a>

1884] The prognosis for these patients is much more favorable than for other pediatric high-grade glioma subtypes.[<a href="#cit/section_1.64">

188564</a>

1886] Refer below for additional discussion of glioblastoma multiforme in infants.</li>

1887<li>

1888<strong>

1889Pilocytic astrocytoma with anaplasia:</strong>

1890 This entity was included in the 2016 WHO classification to describe tumors with histological features of pilocytic astrocytoma, increased mitotic activity, and additional high-grade features. A more recent publication described a cohort of 83 cases with these histological features (referred to as <em>

1891anaplastic astrocytoma with piloid features</em>

1892) that shared a common DNA methylation profile, which is distinct from the methylation profiles of other gliomas. These tumors occurred more often in adults (median age, 41 years), and they harbored frequent deletions of <em class="gene-name">

1893CDKN2A/B</em>

1894, MAPK pathway alterations (most often in the <em class="gene-name">

1895NF1</em>

1896 gene), and mutations or deletions of <em class="gene-name">

1897ATRX</em>

1898. They are associated with a clinical course that is intermediate between pilocytic astrocytoma and <em class="gene-name">

1899IDH</em>

1900&#8211;wild-type glioblastoma.[<a href="#cit/section_1.6">

19016</a>

1902]</li>

1903</ol>

1904</div>

1905</section>

1906<section id="_sm_CDR0000779372_481">

1907<h5 id="_sm_CDR0000779372_481_toc">

1908Other mutations</h5>

1909<p id="_sm_CDR0000779372_472" tabindex="-1">

1910Pediatric glioblastoma multiforme high-grade glioma patients whose tumors lack both histone mutations and <em class="gene-name">

1911IDH1</em>

1912 mutations represent approximately 40% of pediatric glioblastoma multiforme cases.[<a href="#cit/section_1.59">

191359</a>

1914,<a href="#cit/section_1.65">

191565</a>

1916] This is a heterogeneous group, with higher rates of gene amplifications than other pediatric high-grade glioma subtypes. The most commonly amplified genes are <em class="gene-name">

1917PDGFRA</em>

1918, <em class="gene-name">

1919EGFR</em>

1920, <em class="gene-name">

1921CCND/CDK</em>

1922, and <em class="gene-name">

1923MYC/MYCN</em>

1924;[<a href="#cit/section_1.57">

192557</a>

1926,<a href="#cit/section_1.58">

192758</a>

1928] MGMT promoter methylation rates are low in this group.[<a href="#cit/section_1.65">

192965</a>

1930] One report divided this group into three subtypes. The subtype characterized by high rates of <em class="gene-name">

1931MYCN</em>

1932 amplification showed the poorest prognosis, while the subtype characterized by <em class="gene-name">

1933TERT</em>

1934 promoter mutations and <em class="gene-name">

1935EGFR</em>

1936 amplification showed the most favorable prognosis. The third group was characterized by <em class="gene-name">

1937PDGFRA</em>

1938 amplification.[<a href="#cit/section_1.65">

193965</a>

1940]</p>

1941</section>

1942<section id="_sm_CDR0000779372_482">

1943<h5 id="_sm_CDR0000779372_482_toc">

1944High-grade gliomas in infants</h5>

1945<p id="_sm_CDR0000779372_466" tabindex="-1">

1946Infants and young children with high-grade gliomas appear to have tumors with distinctive molecular characteristics when compared with tumors of older children and adults with high-grade gliomas. An indication of this difference was noted with the application of DNA methylation analysis to pediatric high-grade tumors, which found that approximately 7% of pediatric patients with a histological diagnosis of high-grade glioma had tumors with methylation patterns more closely resembling those of low-grade gliomas.[<a href="#cit/section_1.59">

194759</a>

1948] Ten of 16 infants (younger than 1 year) with a high-grade glioma diagnosis were in this methylation array&#8211;defined group.[<a href="#cit/section_1.59">

194959</a>

1950] The 5-year survival rate for patients in this report diagnosed at younger than 1 year exceeded 60%, while the 5-year survival rate for patients aged 1 to 3 years and older was less than 20%.</p>

1951<p id="_sm_CDR0000779372_488" tabindex="-1">

1952Two studies of the molecular characteristics of high-grade gliomas in infants and young children have further defined the distinctive nature of tumors arising in children younger than 1 year. A key finding from both studies is the importance of gene fusions involving tyrosine kinases (e.g., <em class="gene-name">

1953ALK</em>

1954, <em class="gene-name">

1955NTRK1, NTRK2, NTRK3</em>

1956, and <em class="gene-name">

1957ROS1</em>

1958) in patients in this age group. Both studies also found that infants with high-grade gliomas whose tumors have these gene fusions have survival rates much higher than those of older children with high-grade gliomas.[<a href="#cit/section_1.66">

195966</a>

1960,<a href="#cit/section_1.67">

196167</a>

1962]</p>

1963<p id="_sm_CDR0000779372_476" tabindex="-1">

1964The first study presented data for 118 children younger than 1 year with a low-grade or high-grade glioma diagnosis who had tumor tissue available for genomic characterization.[<a href="#cit/section_1.66">

196566</a>

1966] Approximately 75% of the cases were classified as low grade, but the diminished utility of histological classification in this age group was illustrated by the relatively low OS rate for the low-grade cohort (71%) and the relatively favorable survival for the high-grade cohort (55%). Rates of surgical resection were higher for patients with high-grade tumors, a result of many of the low-grade tumors occurring in midline locations while the high-grade tumors were found in supratentorial locations; this finding may also help to explain the relative outcomes for the two groups. Genomic characterization divided the infant glioma population into the following three groups, the first of which included patients with high-grade gliomas:</p>

1967<div class="pdq-content-list">

1968<ul id="_sm_CDR0000779372_477">

1969<li>

1970Group 1 tumors were receptor tyrosine kinase driven and primarily high grade (83%). These tumors harbored lesions in <em class="gene-name">

1971ALK</em>

1972, <em class="gene-name">

1973ROS1</em>

1974, <em class="gene-name">

1975NTRK</em>

1976, and <em class="gene-name">

1977MET</em>

1978. Median age at diagnosis was 3 months, and OS rates were approximately 60%. </li>

1979<li>

1980Group 2 tumors were RAS/MAPK driven and were all hemispheric low-grade gliomas, representing one-fourth of hemispheric gliomas in infants. <em class="gene-name">

1981BRAF</em>

1982 V600E was the most common alteration, followed by <em class="gene-name">

1983FGFR1</em>

1984 alterations and <em class="gene-name">

1985BRAF</em>

1986 fusions. This group had a median age at presentation of 8 months and had the most favorable outcome (10-year OS rate, 93%). </li>

1987<li>

1988Group 3 tumors were RAS/MAPK driven with low-grade histology and midline presentation (approximately 80% optic pathway/hypothalamic gliomas). Most group 3 tumors showed either <em class="gene-name">

1989BRAF</em>

1990 fusions or <em class="gene-name">

1991BRAF</em>

1992 V600E. Median age at diagnosis was 7.5 months. The progression-free survival (PFS) rate at 5-years was approximately 20%, and the OS rate at 10 years was approximately 50% (far inferior to that of optic pathway/hypothalamic gliomas in children aged &gt;1 year).</li>

1993</ul>

1994</div>

1995<p id="_sm_CDR0000779372_489" tabindex="-1">

1996The second study focused on tumors from children younger than 4 years with a pathological diagnosis of WHO grades II, III, and IV gliomas, astrocytomas, or glioneuronal tumors. Among the 191 tumors studied that met inclusion criteria, 61 had methylation profiles consistent with glioma subtypes that occur in older children (e.g., <em class="gene-name">

1997IDH1</em>

1998, diffuse midline glioma K27M-mutant, subependymal giant cell astrocytoma, pleomorphic xanthoastrocytoma, etc.). The remaining 130 cases were termed the <em>

1999intrinsic set</em>

2000 and were the focus of additional molecular characterization:[<a href="#cit/section_1.67">

200167</a>

2002]</p>

2003<div class="pdq-content-list">

2004<ul id="_sm_CDR0000779372_490">

2005<li>

2006The <em>

2007intrinsic set</em>

2008 contained most of the patients diagnosed before age 1 year (49 of 63 patients, 78%) and had a median age of 7.2 months. Tumors were frequently in a superficial hemispheric location, often involving the meninges, and had a well-defined border with adjacent normal brain.</li>

2009<li>

2010The methylation classifier placed most of these cases in either the desmoplastic infantile ganglioglioma/astrocytoma (DIGG/DIA) subgroup or in the infantile hemispheric glioma subgroup.</li>

2011<li>

2012For 41 tumors from the <em>

2013intrinsic set</em>

2014 in which tissue was available for gene panel and RNA sequencing, 25 tumors had fusions involving either <em class="gene-name">

2015ALK</em>

2016 (n = 10), <em class="gene-name">

2017NTRK1</em>

2018 (n = 2), <em class="gene-name">

2019NTRK2</em>

2020 (n = 2), <em class="gene-name">

2021NTRK3</em>

2022 (n = 8), <em class="gene-name">

2023ROS1</em>

2024 (n = 2), or <em class="gene-name">

2025MET</em>

2026 (n = 1). <em class="gene-name">

2027BRAF</em>

2028 mutations (n = 3) were observed in cases that were high scoring by methylation array for the DIGG/DIA or DIGG/DIA-like subgroups. </li>

2029<li>

2030For patients in the <em>

2031intrinsic set</em>

2032, the 5-year survival rate appeared higher for patients whose tumors had gene fusions when compared with patients whose tumors lacked fusions (approximately 80% vs. 60%, respectively); however, both of these groups of patients had much higher survival rates than did other children with high-grade gliomas. </li>

2033</ul>

2034</div>

2035</section>

2036<section id="_sm_CDR0000779372_485">

2037<h5 id="_sm_CDR0000779372_485_toc">

2038Secondary high-grade glioma</h5>

2039<p id="_sm_CDR0000779372_431" tabindex="-1">

2040Childhood secondary high-grade glioma (high-grade glioma that is preceded by a low-grade glioma) is uncommon (2.9% in a study of 886 patients). No pediatric low-grade gliomas with the <em class="gene-name">

2041BRAF-KIAA1549</em>

2042 fusion transformed to a high-grade glioma, whereas low-grade gliomas with the <em class="gene-name">

2043BRAF</em>

2044 V600E mutations were associated with increased risk of transformation. Seven of 18 patients (approximately 40%) with secondary high-grade glioma had <em class="gene-name">

2045BRAF</em>

2046 V600E mutations, with <em class="gene-name">

2047CDKN2A</em>

2048 alterations present in 8 of 14 cases (57%).[<a href="#cit/section_1.31">

204931</a>

2050]</p>

2051</section>

2052<section id="_sm_CDR0000779372_483">

2053<h5 id="_sm_CDR0000779372_483_toc">

2054Neurofibromatosis type 1 (NF1)</h5>

2055<p id="_sm_CDR0000779372_478" tabindex="-1">

2056High-grade gliomas can arise in children with NF1, although low-grade gliomas are much more common. When a high-grade tumor occurs, it is most often in adulthood. Genomic characterization of 23 patients with NF1-associated high-grade gliomas (median age, 38.8 years; 5 patients younger than 18 years) showed higher rates of mutations compared with NF1 patients who had low-grade gliomas (21.5 vs. 6 mutations, respectively).[<a href="#cit/section_1.51">

205751</a>

2058] The vast majority of patients showed <em class="gene-name">

2059NF1</em>

2060 germline mutations, with either loss of heterozygosity or with an inactivating mutation in the second <em class="gene-name">

2061NF1</em>

2062 allele. In contrast to NF1-associated low-grade gliomas, genomic alterations associated with high-grade gliomas were common (<em class="gene-name">

2063CDKN2A</em>

2064 [58%], <em class="gene-name">

2065ATRX</em>

2066 [38%], and <em class="gene-name">

2067TP53</em>

2068 [29%]).[<a href="#cit/section_1.51">

206951</a>

2070]</p>

2071</section>

2072</section>

2073<section id="_sm_CDR0000799873_1851">

2074<h4 id="_sm_CDR0000799873_1851_toc">

2075Molecular features of neuronal and mixed neuronal-glial tumors</h4>

2076<p id="_sm_CDR0000799873_1852" tabindex="-1">

2077Neuronal and mixed neuronal-glial tumors are generally low-grade tumors, with an exception of the grade III anaplastic gangliogliomas. The histologies recognized by the 2016 WHO classification include the following:[<a href="#cit/section_1.2">

20782</a>

2079]</p>

2080<div class="pdq-content-list">

2081<ul id="_sm_CDR0000799873_1853">

2082<li>

2083Dysembryoplastic neuroepithelial tumor.</li>

2084<li>

2085Gangliocytoma.</li>

2086<li>

2087Ganglioglioma.</li>

2088<li>

2089Anaplastic ganglioglioma.</li>

2090<li>

2091Dysplastic cerebellar gangliocytoma (Lhermitte-Duclos disease).</li>

2092<li>

2093Desmoplastic infantile astrocytoma and ganglioglioma.</li>

2094<li>

2095Papillary glioneuronal tumor.</li>

2096<li>

2097Rosette-forming glioneuronal tumor.</li>

2098<li>

2099Diffuse leptomeningeal glioneuronal tumor.</li>

2100<li>

2101Central neurocytoma.</li>

2102<li>

2103Extraventricular neurocytoma.</li>

2104<li>

2105Cerebellar liponeurocytoma.</li>

2106<li>

2107Paraganglioma.</li>

2108</ul>

2109</div>

2110<section id="_sm_CDR0000799873_1867">

2111<h5 id="_sm_CDR0000799873_1867_toc">

2112Dysembryoplastic neuroepithelial tumor (DNET)</h5>

2113<p id="_sm_CDR0000799873_1856" tabindex="-1">

2114DNET presents in children and adults, with the median age at diagnosis in mid-to-late adolescence. It is characterized histopathologically by the presence of columns of oligodendroglial-like cells and cortical ganglion cells floating in mucin.[<a href="#cit/section_1.1">

21151</a>

2116] The temporal lobe is the most common location, and it is associated with drug-refractory epilepsy.[<a href="#cit/section_1.68">

211768</a>

2118,<a href="#cit/section_1.69">

211969</a>

2120] </p>

2121<p id="_sm_CDR0000799873_1876" tabindex="-1">

2122<em class="gene-name">

2123FGFR1</em>

2124 alterations have been reported in 60% to 80% of DNETs, and include <em class="gene-name">

2125FGFR1</em>

2126 activating point mutations, internal tandem duplication of the kinase domain, and activating gene fusions.[<a href="#cit/section_1.43">

212743</a>

2128,<a href="#cit/section_1.70">

212970</a>

2130,<a href="#cit/section_1.71">

213171</a>

2132] <em class="gene-name">

2133BRAF</em>

2134 mutations are uncommon in DNET.</p>

2135</section>

2136<section id="_sm_CDR0000799873_1868">

2137<h5 id="_sm_CDR0000799873_1868_toc">

2138DNET of the septum pellucidum</h5>

2139<p id="_sm_CDR0000799873_1857" tabindex="-1">

2140Septal DNET generally presents with symptoms related to obstructive hydrocephalus.[<a href="#cit/section_1.72">

214172</a>

2142,<a href="#cit/section_1.73">

214373</a>

2144] Septal DNET has an indolent clinical behavior, with most tumors not requiring treatment other than surgery. In a single-institution series that incorporated other literature-reported cases, the median age at presentation was in the adolescent age range.[<a href="#cit/section_1.74">

214574</a>

2146] </p>

2147<p id="_sm_CDR0000799873_1866" tabindex="-1">

2148Mutations that are common in low-grade gliomas (e.g., <em class="gene-name">

2149BRAF</em>

2150 V600E) and in cortical DNETs (<em class="gene-name">

2151FGFR1</em>

2152 mutations) are uncommon in septal DNET.[<a href="#cit/section_1.73">

215373</a>

2154-<a href="#cit/section_1.75">

215575</a>

2156] Instead, mutations in <em class="gene-name">

2157PDGFRA</em>

2158 at the K385 residue typify most cases of septal DNET.</p>

2159<p id="_sm_CDR0000799873_1877" tabindex="-1">

2160A report of the molecular characterization of 18 septal DNETs showed that 14 had a <em class="gene-name">

2161PDGFRA</em>

2162 mutation, with all but one being a mutation at the K385 residue,[<a href="#cit/section_1.74">

216374</a>

2164] which is in the extracellular region of <em class="gene-name">

2165PDGFRA</em>

2166 that mediates the receptor-receptor interaction required for dimerization and activation upon binding of PDGFs. Among the remaining four cases, three had <em class="gene-name">

2167FGFR1</em>

2168 mutations in line with those observed in cortical DNET. A second report observed <em class="gene-name">

2169PDGFRA</em>

2170 mutations at K385 in each of four cases of septal DNET.[<a href="#cit/section_1.75">

217175</a>

2172] Combined, the two reports indicate that septal DNET is a distinct entity characterized by a stereotypic anatomic location and, in most cases, a <em class="gene-name">

2173PDGFRA</em>

2174 mutation. Low-grade glioneuronal tumors with the K385 <em class="gene-name">

2175PDGFRA</em>

2176 mutation have also been identified as arising in the corpus callosum and periventricular white matter of the lateral ventricle, leading to the proposal that <em>

2177myxoid glioneuronal tumor, PDGFRA p.K385-mutant</em>

2178 be considered as a distinct central nervous system (CNS) tumor entity.[<a href="#cit/section_1.76">

217976</a>

2180]</p>

2181</section>

2182<section id="_sm_CDR0000799873_1869">

2183<h5 id="_sm_CDR0000799873_1869_toc">

2184Ganglioglioma</h5>

2185<p id="_sm_CDR0000799873_1855" tabindex="-1">

2186 Ganglioglioma presents during childhood and into adulthood. It most commonly arises in the cerebral cortex and is associated with seizures, but also presents in other sites, including the spinal cord.[<a href="#cit/section_1.68">

218768</a>

2188,<a href="#cit/section_1.77">

218977</a>

2190] </p>

2191<p id="_sm_CDR0000799873_1878" tabindex="-1">

2192The unifying theme for the molecular pathogenesis of ganglioglioma is genomic alterations leading to MAPK pathway activation.[<a href="#cit/section_1.43">

219343</a>

2194,<a href="#cit/section_1.78">

219578</a>

2196] <em class="gene-name">

2197BRAF</em>

2198 alterations are observed in approximately 50% of ganglioglioma cases, with V600E being by far the most common alteration; however, other <em class="gene-name">

2199BRAF</em>

2200 mutations and gene fusions are also observed. Other less commonly altered genes in ganglioglioma include <em class="gene-name">

2201KRAS</em>

2202, <em class="gene-name">

2203FGFR1/2</em>

2204, <em class="gene-name">

2205RAF1</em>

2206, <em class="gene-name">

2207NTRK2</em>

2208, and <em class="gene-name">

2209NF1</em>

2210.[<a href="#cit/section_1.43">

221143</a>

2212,<a href="#cit/section_1.78">

221378</a>

2214]</p>

2215</section>

2216<section id="_sm_CDR0000799873_1870">

2217<h5 id="_sm_CDR0000799873_1870_toc">

2218Desmoplastic infantile astrocytomas (DIA) and desmoplastic infantile gangliogliomas (DIG)</h5>

2219<p id="_sm_CDR0000799873_1858" tabindex="-1">

2220 DIA and DIG most often present in the first year of life and show a characteristic imaging appearance in which a contrast-enhancing solid nodule accompanies a large cystic component.[<a href="#cit/section_1.79">

222179</a>

2222,<a href="#cit/section_1.80">

222380</a>

2224] DIG is more common than DIA,[<a href="#cit/section_1.79">

222579</a>

2226] and by methylation array analysis, both diagnoses cluster together.[<a href="#cit/section_1.81">

222781</a>

2228] Survival outcome is generally favorable with surgical resection.[<a href="#cit/section_1.79">

222979</a>

2230]</p>

2231<p id="_sm_CDR0000799873_1859" tabindex="-1">

2232The most commonly observed genomic alterations in DIA and DIG are <em class="gene-name">

2233BRAF</em>

2234 mutations involving V600; gene fusions involving kinase genes are observed less frequently.</p>

2235<div class="pdq-content-list">

2236<ul id="_sm_CDR0000799873_1860">

2237<li>

2238Among 16 cases confirmed by histology and DNA methylation profiling to be DIA and DIG, <em class="gene-name">

2239BRAF</em>

2240 mutations were observed in seven cases (43.8%): four <em class="gene-name">

2241BRAF</em>

2242 V600E mutations and three <em class="gene-name">

2243BRAF</em>

2244 V600D mutations. One additional case had an <em class="gene-name">

2245EML4-ALK</em>

2246 fusion. <em class="gene-name">

2247BRAF</em>

2248 mutations were present in 4 of 12 (25%) DIG cases (with 3 of 4 mutated cases having <em class="gene-name">

2249BRAF</em>

2250 V600D) and in 3 of 4 (75%) DIA cases (all 3 mutated cases with <em class="gene-name">

2251BRAF</em>

2252 V600E).</li>

2253<li>

2254A study of seven DIG cases found MAPK pathway alterations in four (57%).[<a href="#cit/section_1.82">

225582</a>

2256] Three alterations involved <em class="gene-name">

2257BRAF</em>

2258 (V600E, V600D, and one deletion/insertion centered at V600) and one was a <em class="gene-name">

2259TPM3-NTRK1</em>

2260 in-frame fusion. Notably, the variant allele frequency was low (8%&#8211;27%), suggesting that DIG is characterized by a prominent nonneoplastic component resulting in low clonal driver mutation allele frequencies. </li>

2261<li>

2262Another report also described the <em class="gene-name">

2263BRAF</em>

2264 V600D mutation in a DIG case.[<a href="#cit/section_1.83">

226583</a>

2266] As the V600D mutation is far less common than V600E in other cancers, its detection in multiple DIG cases suggests an association between the mutation and DIG. </li>

2267</ul>

2268</div>

2269</section>

2270<section id="_sm_CDR0000799873_1871">

2271<h5 id="_sm_CDR0000799873_1871_toc">

2272Papillary glioneuronal tumor</h5>

2273<p id="_sm_CDR0000799873_1865" tabindex="-1">

2274Papillary glioneuronal tumor is a low-grade biphasic neoplasm with astrocytic and neuronal differentiation that primarily arises in the supratentorial compartment.[<a href="#cit/section_1.2">

22752</a>

2276] The median age at presentation is in the early 20s, but it can be observed during childhood through adulthood. </p>

2277<p id="_sm_CDR0000799873_1879" tabindex="-1">

2278The primary genomic alteration associated with papillary glioneuronal tumor is a gene fusion, <em class="gene-name">

2279SLC44A1-PRKCA</em>

2280, that is associated with the t(9:17)(q31;q24) translocation.[<a href="#cit/section_1.84">

228184</a>

2282,<a href="#cit/section_1.85">

228385</a>

2284] In one study of 28 cases diagnosed histologically as papillary glioneuronal tumor using methylation arrays, 11 of the cases clustered in a distinctive methylation class, while the remaining cases showed methylation profiles typical for other tumor entities. Molecular analysis of the cases in the distinctive methylation cluster showed that all of them had the <em class="gene-name">

2285SLC44A1-PRKCA</em>

2286 gene fusion except for a single case with a <em class="gene-name">

2287NOTCH1-PRKCA</em>

2288 gene fusion.[<a href="#cit/section_1.86">

228986</a>

2290] This suggests that molecular methods for identifying the presence of a <em class="gene-name">

2291PRKCA</em>

2292 fusion are less susceptible to misclassification in diagnosing papillary glioneuronal tumor than are morphology-based methods.</p>

2293</section>

2294<section id="_sm_CDR0000799873_1872">

2295<h5 id="_sm_CDR0000799873_1872_toc">

2296Rosette-forming glioneuronal tumor (RGNT)</h5>

2297<p id="_sm_CDR0000799873_1861" tabindex="-1">

2298 RGNT presents in adolescents and adults, with tumors generally located infratentorially, although tumors can arise in mesencephalic or diencephalic regions.[<a href="#cit/section_1.87">

229987</a>

2300] The typical histological appearance shows both a glial component and a neurocytic component arranged in rosettes or perivascular pseudorosettes.[<a href="#cit/section_1.2">

23012</a>

2302] Outcome for patients with RGNT is generally favorable, consistent with the WHO grade I designation.[<a href="#cit/section_1.87">

230387</a>

2304] </p>

2305<p id="_sm_CDR0000799873_1880" tabindex="-1">

2306DNA methylation profiling shows that RGNT has a distinct epigenetic profile that distinguishes it from other low-grade glial/glioneuronal tumor entities.[<a href="#cit/section_1.87">

230787</a>

2308] A study of 30 cases of RGNT observed <em class="gene-name">

2309FGFR1</em>

2310 hotspot mutations in all analyzed tumors.[<a href="#cit/section_1.87">

231187</a>

2312] In addition, <em class="gene-name">

2313PIK3CA</em>

2314 activating mutations were concurrently observed in 19 of 30 cases (63%). Missense or damaging mutations in <em class="gene-name">

2315NF1</em>

2316 were identified in 10 of 30 cases (33%), with 7 tumors having mutations in <em class="gene-name">

2317FGFR1</em>

2318, <em class="gene-name">

2319PIK3CA</em>

2320, and <em class="gene-name">

2321NF1</em>

2322. The co-occurrence of mutations that activate both the MAPK pathway and the PI3K pathway makes the mutation profile of RGNT distinctive among astrocytic and glioneuronal tumors.</p>

2323</section>

2324<section id="_sm_CDR0000799873_1873">

2325<h5 id="_sm_CDR0000799873_1873_toc">

2326Diffuse leptomeningeal glioneuronal tumor (DLGNT)</h5>

2327<p id="_sm_CDR0000799873_1862" tabindex="-1">

2328 DLGNT is a rare CNS tumor that has been characterized radiographically by leptomeningeal enhancement on magnetic resonance imaging (MRI) that may involve the posterior fossa, brain stem region, and spinal cord.[<a href="#cit/section_1.88">

232988</a>

2330] Intraparenchymal lesions, when present, typically involve the spinal cord;[<a href="#cit/section_1.88">

233188</a>

2332] localized intramedullary glioneuronal tumors without leptomeningeal dissemination and with histomorphologic, immunophenotypic, and genomic characteristics similar to DLGNT have been reported.[<a href="#cit/section_1.89">

233389</a>

2334] </p>

2335<p id="_sm_CDR0000799873_1881" tabindex="-1">

2336DLGNT showed a distinctive epigenetic profile on DNA methylation arrays, and unsupervised clustering of array data applied to 30 cases defined two subclasses of DLGNT: methylation class (MC)-1 (n = 17) and MC-2 (n = 13).[<a href="#cit/section_1.88">

233788</a>

2338] Of note, many of the array-defined cases had originally been diagnosed as other entities (e.g., primitive neuroectodermal tumors, pilocytic astrocytoma, and anaplastic astrocytoma). Patients with DLGNT-MC-1 were diagnosed at an earlier age than were patients with DLGNT-MC-2 (5 years vs. 14 years, respectively). The 5-year overall survival rate was higher for patients with DLGNT-MC-1 than for those with DLGNT-MC-2 (100% vs. 43%, respectively). Genomic findings from the 30 methylation array&#8211;defined DLGNT cases are provided below:

2339</p>

2340<div class="pdq-content-list">

2341<ul id="_sm_CDR0000799873_1863">

2342<li>

2343All 30 cases showed loss of chromosome 1p, but only 6 of 17 DLGNT-MC-1 cases showed additional gain of chromosome 1q, compared with all cases of DLGNT-MC-2.[<a href="#cit/section_1.88">

234488</a>

2345] A separate report found that chromosome 1q gain was an adverse prognostic factor in patients with DLGNT (including cases with localized disease),[<a href="#cit/section_1.90">

234690</a>

2347] which is consistent with the inferior outcome for patients with DLGNT-MC-2.</li>

2348<li>

2349Co-deletions of 1p/19q were more frequent in the DLGNT-MC-1 group (7 of 13, 54%) than in the DLGNT-MC-2 group (2 of 13, 15%). In contrast to oligodendroglioma, mutations of <em class="gene-name">

2350IDH1</em>

2351 and <em class="gene-name">

2352IDH2</em>

2353 were not identified.[<a href="#cit/section_1.88">

235488</a>

2355]</li>

2356<li>

2357MAPK pathway activation is common in DLGNT cases.[<a href="#cit/section_1.88">

235888</a>

2359] The <em class="gene-name">

2360KIAA1549-BRAF</em>

2361 fusion was present in 11 of 15 DLGNT-MC-1 cases (65%) and in 9 of 13 DLGNT-MC-2 cases (69%). Fusions involving <em class="gene-name">

2362NTRK1/2/3</em>

2363 were present in one case each, and another case had a <em class="gene-name">

2364TRIM33-RAF1</em>

2365 fusion. </li>

2366</ul>

2367</div>

2368</section>

2369<section id="_sm_CDR0000799873_1874">

2370<h5 id="_sm_CDR0000799873_1874_toc">

2371Extraventricular neurocytoma</h5>

2372<p id="_sm_CDR0000799873_1864" tabindex="-1">

2373 Extraventricular neurocytoma is histologically similar to central neurocytoma, consisting of small uniform cells that demonstrate neuronal differentiation, but it arises in the brain parenchyma rather than in association with the ventricular system.[<a href="#cit/section_1.2">

23742</a>

2375] It presents during childhood through adulthood. </p>

2376<p id="_sm_CDR0000799873_1882" tabindex="-1">

2377In a study of 40 tumors histologically classified as extraventricular neurocytoma and subjected to methylation array analysis, only 26 formed a separate cluster distinctive from reference tumors of other histologies.[<a href="#cit/section_1.91">

237891</a>

2379] Among cases with an extraventricular neurocytoma methylation array classification for which genomic characterization could be performed, 11 of 15 (73%) showed rearrangements affecting members of the FGFR family, with <em class="gene-name">

2380FGFR1-TACC1</em>

2381 being the most common alteration.[<a href="#cit/section_1.91">

238291</a>

2383]</p>

2384</section>

2385</section>

2386</section>

2387<section id="_117">

2388<h3 id="_117_toc">

2389Prognosis</h3>

2390<section id="_118">

2391<h4 id="_118_toc">

2392Low-grade astrocytomas</h4>

2393<p id="_119" tabindex="-1">

2394Low-grade astrocytomas (grade I [pilocytic] and grade II) have a relatively favorable prognosis, particularly for well-circumscribed lesions where complete excision may be possible.[<a href="#cit/section_1.12">

239512</a>

2396,<a href="#cit/section_1.92">

239792</a>

2398-<a href="#cit/section_1.95">

239995</a>

2400] Tumor spread, when it occurs, is usually by contiguous extension; dissemination to other CNS sites is uncommon, but does occur.[<a href="#cit/section_1.96">

240196</a>

2402,<a href="#cit/section_1.97">

240397</a>

2404] Although metastasis is uncommon, tumors may be of multifocal origin, especially when associated with NF1.</p>

2405<p id="_235" tabindex="-1">

2406Unfavorable prognostic features for childhood low-grade astrocytomas include the following:[<a href="#cit/section_1.98">

240798</a>

2408-<a href="#cit/section_1.101">

2409101</a>

2410]</p>

2411<div class="pdq-content-list">

2412<ul id="_302">

2413<li>

2414Young age.[<a href="#cit/section_1.101">

2415101</a>

2416]</li>

2417<li>

2418Diffuse histology, especially <em class="gene-name">

2419IDH</em>

2420 mutant.</li>

2421<li>

2422Inability to obtain a complete resection.</li>

2423<li>

2424Diencephalic syndrome.[<a href="#cit/section_1.101">

2425101</a>

2426]</li>

2427<li>

2428Metastases. When metastasis does occur, it is associated with a poorer long-term outcome.[<a href="#cit/section_1.102">

2429102</a>

2430] However, it is increasingly evident that prognosis is largely dependent on specific molecular features integrated with standard pathological grouping.</li>

2431</ul>

2432</div>

2433<p id="_303" tabindex="-1">

2434In patients with pilocytic astrocytoma, elevated MIB-1 labeling index, a marker of cellular proliferative activity, is associated with shortened PFS.[<a href="#cit/section_1.9">

24359</a>

2436] A <em class="gene-name">

2437BRAF-KIAA1549</em>

2438 fusion, found in pilocytic tumors, confers a better clinical outcome.[<a href="#cit/section_1.27">

243927</a>

2440]</p>

2441<p id="_304" tabindex="-1">

2442In children with tumors of the visual pathway, outcome is not only assessed by radiographic disease control or survival but also by visual outcome. Children with isolated optic nerve tumors have a better prognosis than do children with lesions that involve the chiasm or that extend along the optic pathway.[<a href="#cit/section_1.103">

2443103</a>

2444,<a href="#cit/section_1.104">

2445104</a>

2446]; [<a href="#cit/section_1.105">

2447105</a>

2448][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2449Level of evidence C1</a>

2450] Children with NF1 also have a better prognosis, especially when the tumor is found in asymptomatic patients at the time of screening.[<a href="#cit/section_1.106">

2451106</a>

2452] Better visual acuity at diagnosis, older age at diagnosis, and presence of NF1 are associated with better visual outcomes.[<a href="#cit/section_1.107">

2453107</a>

2454]</p>

2455</section>

2456<section id="_120">

2457<h4 id="_120_toc">

2458High-grade astrocytomas</h4>

2459<p id="_305" tabindex="-1">

2460Although high-grade astrocytomas generally carry a poor prognosis in younger patients, those with anaplastic astrocytomas in whom a gross-total resection is possible may fare better,[<a href="#cit/section_1.93">

246193</a>

2462,<a href="#cit/section_1.108">

2463108</a>

2464,<a href="#cit/section_1.109">

2465109</a>

2466] as well as those with non-H3 K27M&#8211;mutant tumors.</p>

2467<p id="_472" tabindex="-1">

2468Molecular subtypes of pediatric glioblastoma multiforme show prognostic significance.[<a href="#cit/section_1.58">

246958</a>

2470] Patients whose tumors have histone K27M mutations have the poorest prognosis, with 3-year survival rates below 5%. In the thalamus, wild-type high-grade gliomas have a somewhat better prognosis than do those harboring an H3.3 mutation. For high-grade gliomas in the thalamus, patients with H3 wild-type tumors have a somewhat better prognosis (2-year overall survival [OS], 71%) than do patients who harbor H3 K27M mutations (2-year OS, 13%).[<a href="#cit/section_1.110">

2471110</a>

2472] Patients whose tumors have <em class="gene-name">

2473IDH1</em>

2474 mutations appear to have the most favorable prognosis among pediatric glioblastoma multiforme cases, while those with histone G34 mutations and those lacking both histone and <em class="gene-name">

2475IDH1</em>

2476 mutations have an intermediate prognosis (3-year OS, approximately 30%). In a multivariate analysis that included both molecular and clinical factors, the presence of gene amplifications and K27M mutations were associated with a poorer prognosis, while the presence of <em class="gene-name">

2477IDH1</em>

2478 mutations was associated with a more favorable prognosis.[<a href="#cit/section_1.58">

247958</a>

2480]</p>

2481</section>

2482</section>

2927 <section id="_33">

2928

2929 <h2>

2930Stage Information for Childhood Astrocytomas</h2>

2931

2932 <div id="_section_33" class="pdq-sections">

2933<p id="_34" tabindex="-1">

2934There is no recognized staging system for childhood astrocytomas. For the purposes of this summary, the treatment of childhood astrocytomas will be described using the following classifications:</p>

2935<div class="pdq-content-list">

2936<ul id="_387">

2937<li>

2938Low-grade astrocytoma&#8212;grades I and II (e.g., pilocytic astrocytomas and diffuse astrocytomas).<div class="pdq-content-list">

2939<ul id="_388" class="list-dash">

2940<li>

2941Newly diagnosed.</li>

2942<li>

2943Progressive/recurrent.</li>

2944</ul>

2945</div>

2946</li>

2947<li>

2948High-grade astrocytoma&#8212;grades III and IV (anaplastic astrocytomas and glioblastoma).<div class="pdq-content-list">

2949<ul id="_389" class="list-dash">

2950<li>

2951Newly diagnosed.</li>

2952<li>

2953Recurrent.</li>

2954</ul>

2955</div>

2956</li>

2957</ul>

2958</div>

2959</div>

2960

2961 </section>

2962

2964

2965 <section id="_35">

2966

2967 <h2>

2968Treatment Option Overview for Childhood Astrocytomas</h2>

2969

2970 <div id="_section_35" class="pdq-sections">

2971<p id="_36" tabindex="-1">

2972Dramatic improvements in survival have been achieved for children and adolescents with cancer. Between 1975 and 2010, childhood cancer mortality decreased by more than 50%.[<a href="#cit/section_3.1">

29731</a>

2974] Many of the improvements in survival in childhood cancer have been made as a result of clinical trials that have attempted to improve on the best available, accepted therapy. Clinical trials in pediatrics are designed to compare new therapy with therapy that is currently accepted as standard. This comparison may be done in a randomized study of two treatment arms or by evaluating a single new treatment and comparing the results with previously obtained results that assessed an existing therapy. Because of the relative rarity of cancer in children, all patients with brain tumors should be considered for entry into a clinical trial. Information about ongoing National Cancer Institute (NCI)&#8211;supported clinical trials is

2975available from the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials">

2976NCI website</a>

2977.</p>

2978<p id="_37" tabindex="-1">

2979To determine and implement optimal treatment, planning by a multidisciplinary team of cancer specialists who have experience treating childhood brain tumors is required. Irradiation of pediatric brain tumors is technically very demanding and should be carried out in centers that have experience in that area to ensure optimal results.</p>

2980<p id="_38" tabindex="-1">

2981Long-term management of patients with brain tumors is complex and requires a multidisciplinary approach. (Refer to the PDQ summary on <a href="/types/childhood-cancers/late-effects-hp-pdq">

2982Late Effects of Treatment for Childhood Cancer</a>

2983 for specific information about the incidence, type, and monitoring of late effects in childhood and adolescent cancer survivors.)</p>

2984<p id="_1801" tabindex="-1">

2985<a href="/types/brain/hp/child-astrocytoma-treament-pdq#_372">

2986Table 4</a>

2987 describes the standard treatment options for low-grade and high-grade childhood astrocytomas.</p>

3115:</td>

3116</tr>

3117<tr>

3118<td>

3119&#8212;<a href="/types/brain/hp/child-astrocytoma-treament-pdq#_327">

3120Radiation therapy</a>

3121 </td>

3122</tr>

3123<tr>

3124<td>

3125&#8212;<a href="/types/brain/hp/child-astrocytoma-treament-pdq#_329">

3126Chemotherapy</a>

3127</td>

3128</tr>

3129<tr>

3130<td rowspan="5">

3131&#160;</td>

3132<td rowspan="5">

3133Recurrent childhood high-grade astrocytomas</td>

3134<td>

3135<a href="/types/brain/hp/child-astrocytoma-treament-pdq#_1874">

3136Surgery</a>

3137 (not considered standard treatment)</td>

3138</tr>

3141<a href="/types/brain/hp/child-astrocytoma-treament-pdq#_1875">

3156BRAF</em>
