3181 <section id="_306">

3182

3183 <h2>

3184Treatment of Childhood Low-Grade Astrocytomas</h2>

3185

3186 <div id="_section_306" class="pdq-sections">

3187<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

3188<h6>

3189In This Section</h6>

3190<ul>

3191<li>

3192<a href="#_312_toc">

3193Observation without Intervention</a>

3194</li>

3195<li>

3196<a href="#_44_toc">

3197Surgery</a>

3198</li>

3199<li>

3200<a href="#_310_toc">

3201Adjuvant Therapy<ul>

3202<li>

3203<a href="#_177_toc">

3204Observation after surgery</a>

3205</li>

3206<li>

3207<a href="#_182_toc">

3208Chemotherapy</a>

3209</li>

3210<li>

3211<a href="#_179_toc">

3212Radiation therapy</a>

3213</li>

3214<li>

3215<a href="#_403_toc">

3216Targeted therapy</a>

3217</li>

3218</ul>

3219</a>

3220</li>

3221<li>

3222<a href="#_1835_toc">

3223Treatment Options Under Clinical Evaluation<ul>

3224<li>

3225<a href="#_TrialSearch_1835_sid_9_toc">

3226Current Clinical Trials</a>

3227</li>

3228</ul>

3229</a>

3230</li>

3231</ul>

3232</nav>

3233<p id="_41" tabindex="-1">

3234To determine and implement optimal management, treatment is often guided by a multidisciplinary team of cancer specialists who have experience treating childhood brain tumors. </p>

3235<p id="_43" tabindex="-1">

3236For children with low-grade optic pathway astrocytomas, treatment options should be considered not only to improve survival but also to stabilize visual function.[<a href="#cit/section_4.1">

32371</a>

3238,<a href="#cit/section_4.2">

32392</a>

3240] </p>

3241<p id="_356" tabindex="-1">

3242Standard treatment options for newly diagnosed childhood low-grade astrocytomas include the following:</p>

3243<div class="pdq-content-list">

3244<ol id="_384">

3245<li>

3246<a href="/types/brain/hp/child-astrocytoma-treament-pdq#_312">

3247Observation without intervention</a>

3248.</li>

3249<li>

3250<a href="/types/brain/hp/child-astrocytoma-treament-pdq#_44">

3251Surgery</a>

3252.</li>

3253<li>

3254<a href="/types/brain/hp/child-astrocytoma-treament-pdq#_310">

3255Adjuvant therapy</a>

3256 (for tumors that are incompletely resected).<div class="pdq-content-list">

3257<ul id="_358">

3258<li>

3259<a href="/types/brain/hp/child-astrocytoma-treament-pdq#_177">

3260Observation after surgery</a>

3261.</li>

3262<li>

3263<a href="/types/brain/hp/child-astrocytoma-treament-pdq#_182">

3264Chemotherapy</a>

3265.</li>

3266<li>

3267<a href="/types/brain/hp/child-astrocytoma-treament-pdq#_179">

3268Radiation therapy</a>

3269.</li>

3270<li>

3271<a href="/types/brain/hp/child-astrocytoma-treament-pdq#_403">

3272Targeted therapy</a>

3273.</li>

3274</ul>

3275</div>

3276</li>

3277</ol>

3278</div>

3279<section id="_312">

3280<h3 id="_312_toc">

3281Observation without Intervention</h3>

3282<p id="_313" tabindex="-1">

3283Observation, in the absence of any intervention, is an option for patients with neurofibromatosis type 1 (NF1) or incidentally found, asymptomatic masses.[<a href="#cit/section_4.3">

32843</a>

3285] Spontaneous regressions of optic pathway gliomas have been reported in children with and without NF1.[<a href="#cit/section_4.4">

32864</a>

3287,<a href="#cit/section_4.5">

32885</a>

3289]</p>

3290</section>

3291<section id="_44">

3292<h3 id="_44_toc">

3293Surgery</h3>

3294<p id="_45" tabindex="-1">

3295Surgical resection is the primary treatment for childhood low-grade astrocytoma [<a href="#cit/section_4.6">

32966</a>

3297,<a href="#cit/section_4.7">

32987</a>

3299] and surgical feasibility is determined by tumor location. In infants and young children, low-grade astrocytomas presenting in the optic chiasm/hypothalamus make surgery difficult; consequently, biopsies are not always done. This is especially true in patients with NF1.[<a href="#cit/section_4.6">

33006</a>

3301] When tumors are associated with NF1, they may be of multifocal origin.</p>

3302<div class="pdq-content-list">

3303<ul id="_307">

3304<li>

3305<strong>

3306Cerebellum:</strong>

3307 Complete or near-complete removal can be obtained in 90% to 95% of patients with pilocytic tumors that occur in the cerebellum.[<a href="#cit/section_4.7">

33087</a>

3309]</li>

3310<li>

3311<strong>

3312Optic nerve:</strong>

3313 For children with isolated optic nerve lesions and progressive symptoms, complete surgical resection, while curative, generally results in blindness in the affected eye. In the absence of retained vision in the affected eye, complete surgical resection may be considered when cosmesis related to proptosis is of concern.</li>

3314<li>

3315<strong>

3316Midline structures (hypothalamus, thalamus, and brain stem):</strong>

3317 Low-grade astrocytomas that occur in midline structures can sometimes be aggressively resected, with resultant long-term disease control.[<a href="#cit/section_4.4">

33184</a>

3319,<a href="#cit/section_4.8">

33208</a>

3321,<a href="#cit/section_4.9">

33229</a>

3323]; [<a href="#cit/section_4.10">

332410</a>

3325][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3326Level of evidence C1</a>

3327] Despite the increasing surgical accessibility of these tumors, such resection may result in significant neurologic sequelae, especially in children younger than 2 years at diagnosis.[<a href="#cit/section_4.11">

332811</a>

3329][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3330Level of evidence C1</a>

3331] Because of the infiltrative nature of some deep-seated lesions, extensive surgical resection may not be appropriate and biopsy only should be considered.[<a href="#cit/section_4.12">

333212</a>

3333][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3334Level of evidence C2</a>

3335]</li>

3336<li>

3337<strong>

3338Cerebrum:</strong>

3339 Circumscribed, grade I hemispheric tumors are often amenable to complete surgical resection. </li>

3340<li>

3341<strong>

3342Spine: </strong>

3343Surgical resection for spinal tumors is generally attempted but it often cannot be completed. In a cohort of 128 patients with primary spinal cord low-grade gliomas, gross-total resection was achieved in a minority of the patients (24 of 128). For the entire cohort, long-term disease control was achieved in about 87% of patients, but subsequent treatment in the form of repeat resection, chemotherapy, and/or radiation therapy was frequently required. Notably, disease progression was common (51 of 128 patients), with late-progression events occurring often. Neurological sequelae and orthopedic complications were common.[<a href="#cit/section_4.13">

334413</a>

3345][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3346Level of evidence C2</a>

3347]</li>

3348</ul>

3349</div>

3350<p id="_400" tabindex="-1">

3351After resection, immediate (within 48 hours of resection per Children&#8217;s Oncology Group [COG] criteria) postoperative magnetic resonance imaging is obtained. Surveillance scans are then obtained periodically for completely resected tumors, although the value following the initial 3- to 6-month postoperative period is uncertain.[<a href="#cit/section_4.14">

335214</a>

3353]; [<a href="#cit/section_4.15">

335415</a>

3355][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3356Level of evidence C2</a>

3357]</p>

3358<p id="_212" tabindex="-1">

3359Factors related to outcome for children with low-grade gliomas treated with surgery followed by observation were identified in a COG study that included 518 evaluable patients.[<a href="#cit/section_4.7">

33607</a>

3361] Overall outcome for the entire group was a 78% progression-free survival (PFS) rate at 8 years and 96% overall survival (OS) rate at 8 years. The following factors were related to prognosis:[<a href="#cit/section_4.7">

33627</a>

3363]</p>

3364<div class="pdq-content-list">

3365<ul id="_213">

3366<li>

3367<strong>

3368Tumor location:</strong>

3369 Children with cerebellar and cerebral tumors showed a higher PFS rate at 8 years compared with patients with midline and chiasmatic tumors (84% &#177; 1.9% vs. 51% &#177; 5.9%, respectively).</li>

3370<li>

3371<strong>

3372Histology:</strong>

3373 Approximately three-fourths of patients had pilocytic astrocytoma; PFS and OS were superior for these patients when compared with children with nonpilocytic tumors.</li>

3374<li>

3375<strong>

3376Extent of resection:</strong>

3377 Patients with gross-total resection had 8-year PFS rates exceeding 90% and OS rates of 99%. By comparison, approximately one-half of patients with any degree of residual tumor (as assessed by operative report and by postoperative imaging) showed disease progression by 8 years, although OS rates exceeded 90%.[<a href="#cit/section_4.7">

33787</a>

3379]

3380<p id="_308" tabindex="-1">

3381The extent of resection necessary for cure is unknown because patients with microscopic and even gross residual tumor after surgery may experience long-term PFS without postoperative therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_4.6">

33826</a>

3383,<a href="#cit/section_4.7">

33847</a>

3385] </p>

3386</li>

3387<li>

3388<strong>

3389Age:</strong>

3390 Younger children (age &lt;5 years) showed higher rates of tumor progression but there was no significant age effect for OS in multivariate analysis. In a retrospective review of a different series of pediatric patients, children younger than 1 year with low-grade glioma demonstrated an inferior PFS compared with children aged 1 year and older.[<a href="#cit/section_4.16">

339116</a>

3392]</li>

3393</ul>

3394</div>

3395<p id="_1945" tabindex="-1">

3396A multivariate analysis examined 100 patients with confirmed diagnoses of World Health Organization (WHO) grade II diffuse gliomas treated in an International Society of Paediatric Oncology (SIOP) study. The extent of glioma resection had the greatest impact on patients' event-free survival (EFS) rates. The 5-year EFS rates were 75% to 76% for patients who underwent a complete or subtotal resection. In comparison, 5-year EFS rates were 56% for patients who had a partial resection and 19% for patients who had a biopsy.[<a href="#cit/section_4.17">

339717</a>

3398][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3399Level of evidence B4</a>

3400]</p>

3401<p id="_214" tabindex="-1">

3402The long-term functional outcome of cerebellar pilocytic astrocytomas is relatively favorable. Full-scale mean intelligence quotients (IQs) of patients with low-grade gliomas treated with surgery alone are close to the normative population. However, long-term medical, psychological, and educational deficits may be present in these patients.[<a href="#cit/section_4.18">

340318</a>

3404]; [<a href="#cit/section_4.19">

340519</a>

3406,<a href="#cit/section_4.20">

340720</a>

3408][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3409Level of evidence C1</a>

3410]</p>

3411</section>

3412<section id="_310">

3413<h3 id="_310_toc">

3414Adjuvant Therapy</h3>

3415<p id="_311" tabindex="-1">

3416Adjuvant therapy following complete resection of a low-grade glioma is generally not required unless there is a subsequent recurrence of disease. Treatment options for patients with incompletely resected tumor must be individualized and may include one or more of the following:</p>

3417<div class="pdq-content-list">

3418<ul id="_401">

3419<li>

3420<a href="/types/brain/hp/child-astrocytoma-treament-pdq#_177">

3421Observation after surgery</a>

3422.</li>

3423<li>

3424<a href="/types/brain/hp/child-astrocytoma-treament-pdq#_182">

3425Chemotherapy</a>

3426. </li>

3427<li>

3428<a href="/types/brain/hp/child-astrocytoma-treament-pdq#_179">

3429Radiation therapy</a>

3430.</li>

3431<li>

3432<a href="/types/brain/hp/child-astrocytoma-treament-pdq#_403">

3433Targeted therapy</a>

3434 (for subependymal giant cell astrocytomas).</li>

3435</ul>

3436</div>

3437<p id="_402" tabindex="-1">

3438A shunt or other cerebrospinal fluid diversion procedure may be needed.</p>

3439<section id="_177">

3440<h4 id="_177_toc">

3441Observation after surgery</h4>

3442<p id="_178" tabindex="-1">

3443In patients in whom a portion of the tumor has been resected, the patient may be observed without further disease-directed treatment, particularly if the pace of tumor regrowth is anticipated to be very slow. Approximately 50% of patients with less-than-gross total resection may have disease that remains progression-free at 5 to 8 years, supporting the observation strategy in selected patients.[<a href="#cit/section_4.7">

34447</a>

3445]</p>

3446</section>

3447<section id="_182">

3448<h4 id="_182_toc">

3449Chemotherapy</h4>

3450<p id="_183" tabindex="-1">

3451Given the long-term side effects associated with radiation therapy, postoperative chemotherapy may be initially recommended. </p>

3452<p id="_184" tabindex="-1">

3453Chemotherapy may result in objective tumor shrinkage and delay the need for radiation therapy in most patients.[<a href="#cit/section_4.21">

345421</a>

3455-<a href="#cit/section_4.24">

345624</a>

3457] Chemotherapy is also an option that may delay or avoid radiation therapy in adolescents with optic nerve pathway gliomas.[<a href="#cit/section_4.25">

345825</a>

3459][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3460Level of evidence C2</a>

3461] Chemotherapy has been shown to shrink tumors in children with hypothalamic gliomas and the diencephalic syndrome, resulting in weight gain in those who respond to treatment.[<a href="#cit/section_4.26">

346226</a>

3463] </p>

3464<p id="_185" tabindex="-1">

3465The most widely used regimens to treat tumor progression or symptomatic nonresectable, low-grade gliomas are the following: </p>

3466<div class="pdq-content-list">

3467<ul id="_411">

3468<li>

3469Carboplatin with or without vincristine.[<a href="#cit/section_4.21">

347021</a>

3471,<a href="#cit/section_4.22">

347222</a>

3473,<a href="#cit/section_4.27">

347427</a>

3475]; [<a href="#cit/section_4.28">

347628</a>

3477][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3478Level of evidence C2</a>

3479]</li>

3480<li>

3481Combination of thioguanine, procarbazine, lomustine, and vincristine (TPCV).[<a href="#cit/section_4.24">

348224</a>

3483]; [<a href="#cit/section_4.29">

348429</a>

3485][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3486Level of evidence A1</a>

3487]</li>

3488</ul>

3489</div>

3490<p id="_412" tabindex="-1">

3491The COG reported the results of a randomized phase III trial (<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00002944">

3492COG-A9952</a>

3493) that treated children younger than 10 years with low-grade chiasmatic/hypothalamic gliomas without NF1 using one of two regimens: carboplatin and vincristine (CV) or TPCV. The 5-year EFS rate was 39% (&#177; 4%) for the CV regimen and 52% (&#177; 5%) for the TPCV regimen. Toxicity rates between the two regimens were relatively comparable.[<a href="#cit/section_4.29">

349429</a>

3495] In the same study, children with NF1 were nonrandomly assigned to receive treatment with CV. The 5-year EFS rate for children with NF1 was markedly better, at 69% (&#177; 4%), than it was for children without NF1 who received CV. In multivariate analysis, NF1 was an independent predictor of better EFS but not OS.[<a href="#cit/section_4.30">

349630</a>

3497] In a separate study that included 100 patients with WHO grade II diffuse gliomas, a subset of patients (n = 16) were treated with carboplatin and vincristine; some patients also received etoposide. This subset of patients had a 5-year PFS rate of 38% when patients with <em class="gene-name">

3498Histone3</em>

3499-mutated tumors were excluded.[<a href="#cit/section_4.17">

350017</a>

3501][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3502Level of evidence B4</a>

3503]</p>

3504<p id="_1809" tabindex="-1">

3505A multicenter, prospective, randomized trial that compared treatment with vincristine/carboplatin with vincristine/carboplatin plus etoposide in children with low-grade glioma failed to demonstrate a difference in PFS and OS between the two regimens.[<a href="#cit/section_4.31">

350631</a>

3507][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810025&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810025&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3508Level of evidence B1</a>

3509]</p>

3510<p id="_186" tabindex="-1">

3511Other chemotherapy approaches have been employed to treat children with progressive or symptomatic nonresectable, low-grade astrocytomas, including the following:</p>

3512<div class="pdq-content-list">

3513<ul id="_484">

3514<li>

3515Multiagent, platinum-based regimens.[<a href="#cit/section_4.22">

351622</a>

3517,<a href="#cit/section_4.23">

351823</a>

3519,<a href="#cit/section_4.32">

352032</a>

3521]; [<a href="#cit/section_4.33">

352233</a>

3523][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3524Level of evidence B4</a>

3525]; [<a href="#cit/section_4.34">

352634</a>

3527][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3528Level of evidence C1</a>

3529] Reported 5-year PFS rates have ranged from approximately 35% to 60% for children receiving platinum-based chemotherapy for optic pathway gliomas,[<a href="#cit/section_4.22">

353022</a>

3531,<a href="#cit/section_4.23">

353223</a>

3533] but most patients ultimately require further treatment. This is particularly true for children who initially present with hypothalamic/chiasmatic gliomas that have neuraxis dissemination.[<a href="#cit/section_4.35">

353435</a>

3535][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3536Level of evidence C2</a>

3537]</li>

3538<li>

3539Vinblastine.[<a href="#cit/section_4.36">

354036</a>

3541,<a href="#cit/section_4.37">

354237</a>

3543]</li>

3544<li>

3545Temozolomide.[<a href="#cit/section_4.38">

354638</a>

3547,<a href="#cit/section_4.39">

354839</a>

3549] </li>

3550</ul>

3551</div>

3552<p id="_187" tabindex="-1">

3553Among children receiving chemotherapy for optic pathway gliomas, those without NF1 have higher rates of disease progression than those with NF1, and infants have higher rates of disease progression than do children older than 1 year.[<a href="#cit/section_4.22">

355422</a>

3555,<a href="#cit/section_4.23">

355623</a>

3557,<a href="#cit/section_4.32">

355832</a>

3559,<a href="#cit/section_4.37">

356037</a>

3561] Visual status (including acuity and field) is an important measure of outcome and response to treatment. Vision function can be impaired; it is variable even in patients with radiographic responses and is often less than optimal. More than one-third of patients <em>

3562successfully</em>

3563 treated with chemotherapy have poor vision in one or both eyes, and some patients lose vision despite radiographic evidence of tumor control (response or stability). In most series, children with sporadic visual pathway gliomas have poorer visual outcomes than do children with NF1.[<a href="#cit/section_4.37">

356437</a>

3565]; [<a href="#cit/section_4.40">

356640</a>

3567,<a href="#cit/section_4.41">

356841</a>

3569][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3570Level of evidence C1</a>

3571] Better initial visual acuity, older age, and absence of postchiasmatic involvement are associated with improved or stable vision after chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_4.42">

357242</a>

3573,<a href="#cit/section_4.43">

357443</a>

3575]</p>

3576</section>

3577<section id="_179">

3578<h4 id="_179_toc">

3579Radiation therapy</h4>

3580<p id="_180" tabindex="-1">

3581Radiation therapy is usually reserved until progressive disease is documented [<a href="#cit/section_4.44">

358244</a>

3583,<a href="#cit/section_4.45">

358445</a>

3585] and may be further delayed through the use of chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_4.21">

358621</a>

3587,<a href="#cit/section_4.22">

358822</a>

3589]</p>

3590<p id="_483" tabindex="-1">

3591For children with low-grade gliomas for whom radiation therapy is indicated, approaches that contour the radiation distribution to the tumor and avoid normal brain tissue (<a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=270731&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=NotSet" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000270731&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=NotSet'); return(false);">

35923-D conformal radiation therapy</a>

3593, <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=335073&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=NotSet" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000335073&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=NotSet'); return(false);">

3594intensity-modulated radiation therapy (IMRT)</a>

3595, <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=44464&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=NotSet" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000044464&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=NotSet'); return(false);">

3596stereotactic radiation therapy</a>

3597, and <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=534234&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=NotSet" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000534234&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=NotSet'); return(false);">

3598proton radiation therapy [charged-particle radiation therapy]</a>

3599) all appear effective and may potentially reduce the acute and long-term toxicities associated with these modalities.[<a href="#cit/section_4.46">

360046</a>

3601,<a href="#cit/section_4.47">

360247</a>

3603]; [<a href="#cit/section_4.48">

360448</a>

3605][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3606Level of evidence C2</a>

3607] Radiation doses of 54 Gy in 1.8 Gy fractions are typically used.[<a href="#cit/section_4.49">

360849</a>

3609,<a href="#cit/section_4.50">

361050</a>

3611] In a prospective study of 174 patients treated with proton therapy, the 5-year actuarial rate of local control was 85% (95% confidence interval [CI], 78%&#8211;90%), the PFS rate was 84% (95% CI, 77%&#8211;89%), and the OS rate was 92% (95% CI, 85%&#8211;95%). Brain stem and spinal cord tumor locations and a dose of 54 Gy relative biological effectiveness (RBE) or less were associated with inferior local control (<em>

3612P</em>

3613 &lt; .01 for both).[<a href="#cit/section_4.51">

361451</a>

3615] In a separate study that included 100 patients with WHO grade II diffuse gliomas, a subset of patients (n = 16) were treated with radiation therapy. These patients had a 5-year PFS rate of 74% when patients with <em class="gene-name">

3616Histone3</em>

3617-mutated tumors were excluded.[<a href="#cit/section_4.17">

361817</a>

3619][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3620Level of evidence B4</a>

3621]</p>

3622<p id="_1866" tabindex="-1">

3623Subsequent to radiation therapy administration, care must be taken to distinguish radiation-induced imaging changes, termed <em>

3624pseudoprogression</em>

3625 or <em>

3626spurious progression</em>

3627,[<a href="#cit/section_4.52">

362852</a>

3629] from disease progression. The peak time to radiation therapy&#8211;induced imaging changes, often presenting as an apparent enlargement of the irradiated mass, is 4 to 6 months, but they can manifest even later.[<a href="#cit/section_4.53">

363053</a>

3631-<a href="#cit/section_4.56">

363256</a>

3633]; [<a href="#cit/section_4.57">

363457</a>

3635][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3636Level of evidence B4</a>

3637]; [<a href="#cit/section_4.58">

363858</a>

3639][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3640Level of evidence B4</a>

3641]; [<a href="#cit/section_4.59">

364259</a>

3643][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3644Level of evidence C2</a>

3645]; [<a href="#cit/section_4.60">

364660</a>

3647][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3648Level of evidence C2</a>

3649]; [<a href="#cit/section_4.12">

365012</a>

3651,<a href="#cit/section_4.61">

365261</a>

3653][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3654Level of evidence C2</a>

3655] In a report of 83 patients with low-grade astrocytomas, pseudoprogression was more common after radiation doses of higher than 50.4 Gy (RBE) (hazard ratio [HR], 2.61; <em>

3656P</em>

3657 = .16). Pseudoprogression was also more common after proton radiation therapy than after photon IMRT (HR, 2.15; <em>

3658P</em>

3659 = .048), presumably because of increased effects on the vasculature. Patients with pilocytic histology had lower rates of pseudoprogression than those with nonpilocytic low-grade gliomas (HR, 0.47; <em>

3660P</em>

3661 = .037). There was no association with overall disease control.[<a href="#cit/section_4.52">

366252</a>

3663]</p>

3664<p id="_314" tabindex="-1">

3665Radiation therapy results in long-term radiographic disease control for most children with chiasmatic and posterior pathway chiasmatic gliomas; however, despite radiographic control, visual outcomes are variable.</p>

3666<div class="pdq-content-list">

3667<ul id="_1926">

3668<li>

3669 A study from St. Jude Children&#8217;s Research Hospital reported on long-term visual acuity outcomes after radiation therapy. For the worse eye, the 5-year cumulative incidence of visual acuity decline was 17.9% and improvement was 13.5%. For the better eye, the 5-year cumulative incidence of visual acuity decline was 11.5% and improvement was 10.6%. After radiation therapy, most patients had stabilization of their vision. Visual change after radiation therapy was most likely to occur within 2 years, supporting the importance of visual assessments during this period.[<a href="#cit/section_4.62">

367062</a>

3671]</li>

3672<li>

3673Another study of 38 patients (mean age, 3 years; median follow-up, 8.5 years) with optic pathway gliomas treated between 2000 and 2018 complemented the previous data on preservation of long-term visual acuity. For patients treated with early radiation therapy (either up-front or as first salvage), blindness-free survival rates were 100% at 5 and 8 years. In comparison, blindness-free survival rates were 81% at 5 years and 60% at 8 years for patients treated primarily with chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_4.63">

367463</a>

3675]</li>

3676<li>

3677 Other sequelae include intellectual and endocrinologic deterioration, cerebrovascular damage, late death, and possibly an increased risk of secondary tumors.[<a href="#cit/section_4.64">

367864</a>

3679-<a href="#cit/section_4.66">

368066</a>

3681]; [<a href="#cit/section_4.58">

368258</a>

3683][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3684Level of evidence B4</a>

3685] A population-based study identified radiation therapy as the most significant risk factor associated with late mortality, although the patients who required radiation therapy may have reflected a higher-risk population.[<a href="#cit/section_4.66">

368666</a>

3687]</li>

3688</ul>

3689</div>

3690<p id="_1927" tabindex="-1">

3691Because the management of unresectable low-grade gliomas and glioneuronal tumors is controversial, the St. Jude Children&#8217;s Research Hospital assessed negative prognostic factors in 150 children (median age, 8 years; range, 1.2&#8211;20 years) who were treated with radiation therapy and followed for a median of 11.4 years (range, 0.24&#8211;29.4 years). Recursive positioning analysis yielded low-risk and high-risk prognostic groups, with a 10-year OS rate of 95.6% (low-risk group) versus 76.4% (high-risk group). Low-risk tumors included pilocytic astrocytoma/ganglioglioma located outside of the midbrain/thalamus, while high-risk tumors included diffuse astrocytoma or those located in the midbrain/thalamus. Within the high-risk group of patients, delayed radiation therapy (defined as after at least one line of chemotherapy) was associated with a decrement in OS.[<a href="#cit/section_4.67">

369267</a>

3693]</p>

3694<p id="_315" tabindex="-1">

3695Children with NF1 may be at higher risk of radiation-associated secondary tumors and morbidity resulting from vascular changes. Radiation therapy and alkylating agents are used as last resorts for these patients, given the theoretically heightened risk of inducing neurologic toxic effects and second malignancy.[<a href="#cit/section_4.68">

369668</a>

3697] </p>

3698</section>

3699<section id="_403">

3700<h4 id="_403_toc">

3701Targeted therapy</h4>

3702<p id="_404" tabindex="-1">

3703For children with symptomatic subependymal giant cell astrocytomas (SEGAs), agents that inhibit mammalian target of rapamycin (mTOR) (e.g., everolimus and sirolimus) have been studied.</p>

3704<p id="_485" tabindex="-1">

3705Evidence (treatment of SEGA with an mTOR inhibitor):</p>

3706<div class="pdq-content-list">

3707<ol id="_486">

3708<li>

3709Small series have shown significant reductions in the size of these tumors after administration of everolimus or sirolimus, often eliminating the need for surgery.[<a href="#cit/section_4.69">

371069</a>

3711]; [<a href="#cit/section_4.70">

371270</a>

3713][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3714Level of evidence B4</a>

3715]; [<a href="#cit/section_4.71">

371671</a>

3717][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3718Level of evidence C3</a>

3719]; [<a href="#cit/section_4.72">

372072</a>

3721][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3722Level of evidence C1</a>

3723]</li>

3724<li>

3725A multicenter, phase III, placebo-controlled trial of 117 patients confirmed these earlier findings.[<a href="#cit/section_4.73">

372673</a>

3727][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810031&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810031&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3728Level of evidence B3</a>

3729]<div class="pdq-content-list">

3730<ul id="_1880">

3731<li>

373235% of the patients in the everolimus group had at least a 50% reduction in the size of the SEGA, versus no reduction in the placebo group.</li>

3733</ul>

3734</div>

3735</li>

3736<li>

3737In a study of patients who were treated with everolimus for 5 years, the following results were observed:[<a href="#cit/section_4.74">

373874</a>

3739]<div class="pdq-content-list">

3740<ul id="_1881">

3741<li>

3742A reduction in the size of the mass was observed in about 50% of patients; in many cases, the reduction was sustained. </li>

3743<li>

3744These patients also had a reduction in seizure frequency.</li>

3745</ul>

3746</div>

3747</li>

3748</ol>

3749</div>

3750</section>

3751</section>

3752<section id="_1835">

3753<h3 id="_1835_toc">

3754Treatment Options Under Clinical Evaluation</h3>

3755<p id="_1836" tabindex="-1">

3756Early-phase therapeutic trials may be available for selected patients. These trials may be available via the <a href="https://www.childrensoncologygroup.org/" title="https://www.childrensoncologygroup.org/">

3757COG</a>

3758, the <a href="http://www.pbtc.org/" title="http://www.pbtc.org/">

3759Pediatric Brain Tumor Consortium</a>

3760, or other entities. Information about National Cancer Institute (NCI)&#8211;supported clinical trials can be found on the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search">

3761NCI website</a>

3762. For information about clinical trials sponsored by other organizations, refer to the <a href="https://clinicaltrials.gov/" title="https://clinicaltrials.gov/">

3763ClinicalTrials.gov website</a>

3764.</p>

3765<p id="_1837" tabindex="-1">

3766The following are examples of national and/or institutional clinical trials that are currently being conducted:</p>

3767<div class="pdq-content-list">

3768<ul id="_1838">

3769<li>

3770<strong>

3771<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT03871257">

3772ACNS1831 (NCT03871257</a>

3773)</strong>

3774 (A Study of the Drugs Selumetinib Versus Carboplatin/Vincristine in Patients With NF1 and Low-Grade Glioma)<strong>

3775:</strong>

3776 This phase III trial investigates the use of selumetinib compared with the standard treatment of CV for treating patients with NF1-associated low-grade gliomas, and improving vision in patients with low-grade gliomas of the optic pathway (vision nerves). </li>

3777<li>

3778<strong>

3779<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT04166409">

3780ACNS1833 (NCT04166409)</a>

3781</strong>

3782 (A Study of the Drugs Selumetinib Versus Carboplatin and Vincristine in Patients With Low-Grade Glioma)<strong>

3783:</strong>

3784 This phase III trial compares the effect of selumetinib with the standard of care treatment using carboplatin and vincristine in treating patients with newly diagnosed or previously untreated low-grade glioma that does not have a <em class="gene-name">

3785BRAF</em>

3786 V600E mutation and is not associated with systemic NF1.</li>

3787</ul>

3788</div>

3789<section id="_TrialSearch_1835_sid_9">

3790<h4 id="_TrialSearch_1835_sid_9_toc">

3791Current Clinical Trials</h4>

3792<p id="_TrialSearch_1835_22" tabindex="-1">

3793Use our <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search/advanced" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search/advanced">

3794advanced clinical trial search</a>

3795 to find NCI-supported cancer clinical trials that are now enrolling patients. The search can be narrowed by location of the trial, type of treatment, name of the drug, and other criteria. <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/">

3796General information</a>

3797 about clinical trials is also available.</p>

4105

4106 <section id="_93">

4107

4108 <h2>

4109Treatment of Progressive/Recurrent Childhood Low-Grade Astrocytomas</h2>

4110

4111 <div id="_section_93" class="pdq-sections">

4112<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

4113<h6>

4114In This Section</h6>

4115<ul>

4116<li>

4117<a href="#_361_toc">

4118Second Surgery</a>

4119</li>

4120<li>

4121<a href="#_363_toc">

4122Radiation Therapy</a>

4123</li>

4124<li>

4125<a href="#_366_toc">

4126Chemotherapy</a>

4127</li>

4128<li>

4129<a href="#_469_toc">

4130Targeted Therapy With or Without Chemotherapy</a>

4131</li>

4132<li>

4133<a href="#_1912_toc">

4134Other Targeted Therapies</a>

4135</li>

4136<li>

4137<a href="#_165_toc">

4138Treatment Options Under Clinical Evaluation<ul>

4139<li>

4140<a href="#_TrialSearch_165_sid_16_toc">

4141Current Clinical Trials</a>

4142</li>

4143</ul>

4144</a>

4145</li>

4146</ul>

4147</nav>

4148<p id="_1868" tabindex="-1">

4149To determine and implement optimal management, treatment is often guided by a multidisciplinary team of cancer specialists who have experience treating childhood brain tumors. </p>

4150<p id="_1869" tabindex="-1">

4151For children with low-grade optic pathway astrocytomas, treatment options should be considered not only to improve survival but also to stabilize visual function.[<a href="#cit/section_5.1">

41521</a>

4153,<a href="#cit/section_5.2">

41542</a>

4155] </p>

4156<p id="_94" tabindex="-1">

4157Childhood low-grade astrocytomas may progress or recur many years after initial presentation and treatment. </p>

4158<p id="_413" tabindex="-1">

4159An individual plan needs to be tailored on the basis of the following:</p>

4160<div class="pdq-content-list">

4161<ul id="_414">

4162<li>

4163Patient age.</li>

4164<li>

4165Tumor location.</li>

4166<li>

4167Prior treatment.</li>

4168</ul>

4169</div>

4170<p id="_415" tabindex="-1">

4171Recurrent disease is usually at the primary tumor site, although multifocal or widely disseminated disease to other intracranial sites and to the spinal leptomeninges has been documented.[<a href="#cit/section_5.3">

41723</a>

4173,<a href="#cit/section_5.4">

41744</a>

4175] Most children whose low-grade diffuse fibrillary astrocytomas recur will harbor low-grade lesions; however, transformation into a higher grade tumor is possible. Surveillance imaging will frequently identify asymptomatic recurrences.[<a href="#cit/section_5.5">

41765</a>

4177] At the time of recurrence, a complete evaluation to determine the extent of the relapse is indicated. </p>

4178<p id="_359" tabindex="-1">

4179Standard treatment options for progressive/recurrent childhood low-grade astrocytomas include the following:</p>

4180<div class="pdq-content-list">

4181<ol id="_385">

4182<li>

4183<a href="/types/brain/hp/child-astrocytoma-treament-pdq#_361">

4184Second surgery</a>

4185.</li>

4186<li>

4187<a href="/types/brain/hp/child-astrocytoma-treament-pdq#_363">

4188Radiation therapy</a>

4189.</li>

4190<li>

4191<a href="/types/brain/hp/child-astrocytoma-treament-pdq#_366">

4192Chemotherapy</a>

4193.</li>

4194<li>

4195<a href="/types/brain/hp/child-astrocytoma-treament-pdq#_469">

4196Targeted therapy with or without chemotherapy</a>

4197.</li>

4198<li>

4199<a href="/types/brain/hp/child-astrocytoma-treament-pdq#_1912">

4200Other targeted therapies</a>

4201.</li>

4202</ol>

4203</div>

4204<section id="_361">

4205<h3 id="_361_toc">

4206Second Surgery</h3>

4207<p id="_362" tabindex="-1">

4208Patients with low-grade astrocytomas who relapse after being treated with surgery alone may be candidates for another surgical resection.[<a href="#cit/section_5.6">

42096</a>

4210] The need for surgical intervention must be individualized on the basis of the following: </p>

4211<div class="pdq-content-list">

4212<ul id="_1871">

4213<li>

4214Initial tumor type.</li>

4215<li>

4216Length of time between initial treatment and the reappearance of the mass lesion.</li>

4217<li>

4218Clinical picture.</li>

4219</ul>

4220</div>

4221<p id="_1872" tabindex="-1">

4222Utility of second surgery is impacted by site of recurrence and the probability of obtaining a near-total resection/gross-total resection without significant neurologic injury.[<a href="#cit/section_5.7">

42237</a>

4224]</p>

4225</section>

4226<section id="_363">

4227<h3 id="_363_toc">

4228Radiation Therapy</h3>

4229<p id="_365" tabindex="-1">

4230The rationale for the use of radiation therapy is essentially the same when utilized as first-line therapy or at the time of recurrence (refer to the <a href="/types/brain/hp/child-astrocytoma-treament-pdq#_179">

4231Radiation therapy</a>

4232 subsection of the <a href="/types/brain/hp/child-astrocytoma-treament-pdq#_306">

4233Treatment of Childhood Low-Grade Astrocytomas</a>

4234 section of this summary). If the child has never received radiation therapy, local radiation therapy may be a treatment option, although chemotherapy in lieu of radiation may be considered, depending on the child's age and the extent and location of the tumor.[<a href="#cit/section_5.8">

42358</a>

4236][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4237Level of evidence C1</a>

4238]; [<a href="#cit/section_5.9">

42399</a>

4240][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4241Level of evidence C2</a>

4242] </p>

4243<p id="_405" tabindex="-1">

4244For children with low-grade gliomas for whom radiation therapy is indicated, <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=270731&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=NotSet" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000270731&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=NotSet'); return(false);">

4245conformal radiation therapy</a>

4246 approaches appear effective and offer the potential for reducing the acute and long-term toxicities associated with this modality.[<a href="#cit/section_5.10">

424710</a>

4248-<a href="#cit/section_5.13">

424913</a>

4250]</p>

4251</section>

4252<section id="_366">

4253<h3 id="_366_toc">

4254Chemotherapy</h3>

4255<p id="_367" tabindex="-1">

4256If there is recurrence at an unresectable site, chemotherapy should be considered.</p>

4257<p id="_368" tabindex="-1">

4258Chemotherapy may result in relatively long-term disease control.[<a href="#cit/section_5.14">

425914</a>

4260,<a href="#cit/section_5.15">

426115</a>

4262] The choice of regimen depends on whether previous chemotherapy has been utilized. Numerous options can be considered, including carboplatin and vincristine (CV); thioguanine, procarbazine, lomustine, and vincristine (TPCV); vinblastine alone; temozolomide alone; or temozolomide in combination with carboplatin and vincristine.[<a href="#cit/section_5.14">

426314</a>

4264-<a href="#cit/section_5.17">

426517</a>

4266]</p>

4267</section>

4268<section id="_469">

4269<h3 id="_469_toc">

4270Targeted Therapy With or Without Chemotherapy</h3>

4271<p id="_493" tabindex="-1">

4272Antitumor activity has also been observed for bevacizumab given in combination with irinotecan, which, in some cases, also results in clinical or visual improvement.[<a href="#cit/section_5.18">

427318</a>

4274]</p>

4275<p id="_494" tabindex="-1">

4276Evidence (targeted therapy [bevacizumab]):</p>

4277<div class="pdq-content-list">

4278<ol id="_495">

4279<li>

4280In a phase II study of bevacizumab plus irinotecan for children with recurrent low-grade gliomas, the following results were observed:[<a href="#cit/section_5.19">

428119</a>

4282]<div class="pdq-content-list">

4283<ul id="_1882">

4284<li>

4285Sustained partial responses were observed in only two patients (5.7%).</li>

4286<li>

4287The 6-month progression-free survival (PFS) rate was 85.4% (standard error [SE] &#177; 5.96%).</li>

4288<li>

4289The 2-year PFS rate was 47.8% (SE &#177; 9.27%).</li>

4290</ul>

4291</div>

4292</li>

4293<li>

4294A pilot study of 14 patients with recurrent low-grade gliomas also evaluated bevacizumab-based therapies and observed the following:[<a href="#cit/section_5.20">

429520</a>

4296][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4297Level of evidence C2</a>

4298]; [<a href="#cit/section_5.21">

429921</a>

4300][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4301Level of evidence C3</a>

4302] <div class="pdq-content-list">

4303<ul id="_1883">

4304<li>

4305Objective responses were seen in 12 patients (86%).</li>

4306<li>

4307No patients progressed on therapy (median treatment duration, 12 months), but 13 of 14 progressed after stopping bevacizumab at a median of 5 months.</li>

4308</ul>

4309</div>

4310</li>

4311<li>

4312Bevacizumab has also been employed for children with low-grade gliomas and symptomatic radiation-induced tumor enlargement.[<a href="#cit/section_5.22">

431322</a>

4314,<a href="#cit/section_5.23">

431523</a>

4316]<div class="pdq-content-list">

4317<ul id="_1884">

4318<li>

4319Treatment with bevacizumab produced radiographic improvement (five of five patients) and allowed weaning off steroids (four of four patients).</li>

4320</ul>

4321</div>

4322</li>

4323</ol>

4324</div>

4325<p id="_470" tabindex="-1">

4326With the identification of <em class="gene-name">

4327BRAF</em>

4328 mutations driving a significant proportion of low-grade gliomas, inhibition of various elements of this molecular pathway (e.g., MEK and BRAF) are actively being tested in ongoing clinical trials, with early reports suggesting substantial activity. While first-generation BRAF inhibitors like vemurafenib and dabrafenib are active against <em class="gene-name">

4329BRAF</em>

4330 V600E&#8211;mutated tumors, they are contraindicated for tumors with <em class="gene-name">

4331BRAF</em>

4332 gene fusions because of the potential for paradoxical activation of the MAPK pathway.[<a href="#cit/section_5.24">

433324</a>

4334,<a href="#cit/section_5.25">

433525</a>

4336]</p>

4337<p id="_1821" tabindex="-1">

4338Studies of BRAF and MEK inhibitors include the following:</p>

4339<div class="pdq-content-list">

4340<ol id="_1825">

4341<li>

4342For patients whose tumors have <em class="gene-name">

4343BRAF</em>

4344 V600E mutations, the focus of clinical research efforts is on the evaluation of BRAF inhibitors in combination with MEK inhibitors. Such combinations are approved for the treatment of adult cancers with <em class="gene-name">

4345BRAF</em>

4346 V600E mutations and are more effective than either BRAF inhibitors or MEK inhibitors used as single agents.[<a href="#cit/section_5.26">

434726</a>

4348] <div class="pdq-content-list">

4349<ul id="_1826">

4350<li>

4351Results on the use of the BRAF V600E inhibitor dabrafenib demonstrated a 44% overall response rate (1 complete response and 13 partial responses) by central review in children with <em class="gene-name">

4352BRAF</em>

4353 V600&#8211;mutated relapsed or refractory low-grade gliomas. The median duration of response was 26 months. Disease control (complete response plus partial response plus stable disease) was 78%. The therapy was well tolerated, although 91% of patients had some side effects such as fatigue (34%), rash (31%), and pyrexia (28%). Nine of 32 patients had grade 3 to grade 4 toxicities, 10 patients required dose modifications, and 2 patients discontinued treatment, including 1 child who had disseminated intravascular coagulation with hypertension. In this pediatric study, no cases of squamous cell carcinoma of the skin or keratoacanthoma were encountered.[<a href="#cit/section_5.27">

435427</a>

4355]</li>

4356<li>

4357Case reports have also documented activity for BRAF inhibitors such as vemurafenib and for BRAF inhibitors in combination with MEK inhibitors for children, adolescents, and young adults with <em class="gene-name">

4358BRAF</em>

4359 V600&#8211;mutated low-grade gliomas.[<a href="#cit/section_5.28">

436028</a>

4361-<a href="#cit/section_5.32">

436232</a>

4363]</li>

4364</ul>

4365</div>

4366</li>

4367<li>

4368The MEK inhibitor selumetinib has been studied in a phase I/II clinical trial for children with low-grade gliomas (<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT01089101">

4369PBTC-029 [NCT01089101]</a>

4370). <div class="pdq-content-list">

4371<ol id="_1885" class="lower-alpha">

4372<li>

4373The phase I component of the PBTC-029 trial showed the following results:[<a href="#cit/section_5.33">

437433</a>

4375]<div class="pdq-content-list">

4376<ul id="_1886">

4377<li>

4378Selumetinib was tolerated at a daily dose of 25 mg/m<span class="sup">

43792</span>

4380.</li>

4381<li>

4382The most common adverse events leading to patient discontinuation of treatment were rash, paronychia, and asymptomatic creatine phosphokinase (CPK) elevation.</li>

4383</ul>

4384</div>

4385</li>

4386<li>

4387Stratum 1 of the phase II component of this trial was for patients with <em class="gene-name">

4388BRAF</em>

4389 genomic alterations.[<a href="#cit/section_5.34">

439034</a>

4391,<a href="#cit/section_5.35">

439235</a>

4393]<div class="pdq-content-list">

4394<ul id="_1887">

4395<li>

4396Nine of 25 patients (36%) achieved a partial response, with responses occurring for both <em class="gene-name">

4397BRAF</em>

4398 V600E patients and for patients with <em class="gene-name">

4399BRAF</em>

4400 gene fusions.</li>

4401<li>

4402 The 2-year PFS rate was 70% for stratum 1 patients.</li>

4403</ul>

4404</div>

4405</li>

4406<li>

4407Stratum 3 of the phase II component of this trial was for patients with NF1-associated low-grade gliomas.[<a href="#cit/section_5.35">

440835</a>

4409]<div class="pdq-content-list">

4410<ul id="_1888">

4411<li>

4412The 2-year event-free survival rate for this group was 96%.</li>

4413<li>

441410 of 25 patients (40%) achieved partial responses.</li>

4415</ul>

4416</div>

4417</li>

4418</ol>

4419</div>

4420<p id="_1889" tabindex="-1">

4421The most common toxicities across all strata were grade 1 and grade 2 CPK elevation, diarrhea, hypoalbuminemia, elevated aspartate aminotransferase (AST), and rash. Rare grade 3 and grade 4 toxicities included elevated CPK, rash, neutropenia, emesis, and paronychia.</p>

4422</li>

4423</ol>

4424</div>

4425</section>

4426<section id="_1912">

4427<h3 id="_1912_toc">

4428Other Targeted Therapies</h3>

4429<p id="_1913" tabindex="-1">

4430In a series of 23 patients with recurrent low-grade gliomas, everolimus demonstrated modest activity, with a 2-year PFS rate of 39% and an overall survival rate of 93%.[<a href="#cit/section_5.36">

443136</a>

4432] A companion study completed by the Neurofibromatosis Clinical Trials Consortium evaluated 23 children with NF1 and progressive low-grade gliomas. Of the 22 evaluable patients, 15 demonstrated either a partial response or tumor stabilization, 10 of whom remained free of progression for a median follow-up of 33 months.[<a href="#cit/section_5.37">

443337</a>

4434]</p>

4435</section>

4436<section id="_165">

4437<h3 id="_165_toc">

4438Treatment Options Under Clinical Evaluation</h3>

4439<p id="_166" tabindex="-1">

4440Early-phase therapeutic trials may be available for selected patients. These trials may be available via the <a href="https://www.childrensoncologygroup.org/" title="https://www.childrensoncologygroup.org/">

4441COG</a>

4442, the <a href="http://www.pbtc.org/" title="http://www.pbtc.org/">

4443Pediatric Brain Tumor Consortium</a>

4444, or other entities. Information about National Cancer Institute (NCI)&#8211;supported clinical trials can be found on the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search">

4445NCI website</a>

4446. For information about clinical trials sponsored by other organizations, refer to the <a href="https://clinicaltrials.gov/" title="https://clinicaltrials.gov/">

4447ClinicalTrials.gov website</a>

4448.</p>

4449<p id="_1799" tabindex="-1">

4450The following are examples of national and/or institutional clinical trials that are currently being conducted:</p>

4451<div class="pdq-content-list">

4452<ul id="_383">

4453<li>

4454<strong>

4455<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT04576117">

4456ACNS1931 (NCT04576117)</a>

4457</strong>

4458 (A Study to Compare Treatment With the Drug Selumetinib Alone Versus Selumetinib and Vinblastine in Patients With Recurrent or Progressive Low-Grade Glioma)<strong>

4459:</strong>

4460 This phase III trial is investigating the best dose of vinblastine in combination with selumetinib and the benefit of adding vinblastine to selumetinib compared with selumetinib alone in treating children and young adults with progressive or recurrent low-grade glioma.</li>

4461<li>

4462<strong>

4463<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT03155620">

4464APEC1621 (NCT03155620)</a>

4465</strong>

4466 (Pediatric MATCH: Targeted Therapy Directed by Genetic Testing in Treating Pediatric Patients with Relapsed or Refractory Advanced Solid Tumors, Non-Hodgkin Lymphomas, or Histiocytic Disorders)<strong>

4467:</strong>

4468 NCI-COG Pediatric Molecular Analysis for Therapeutic Choice (MATCH), referred to as Pediatric MATCH, will match targeted agents with specific molecular changes identified in a patient's tumor (refractory or recurrent). Children and adolescents aged 1 to 21 years are eligible for the trial.<p id="_1807" tabindex="-1">

4469Patients with tumors that have molecular variants addressed by treatment arms included in the trial will be offered treatment on Pediatric MATCH. Additional information can be obtained on the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/nci-supported/pediatric-match" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/nci-supported/pediatric-match">

4470NCI website</a>

4471 and <a href="https://clinicaltrials.gov/ct2/show/NCT03155620" title="https://clinicaltrials.gov/ct2/show/NCT03155620">

4472ClinicalTrials.gov website</a>

4473.</p>

4474

4475</li>

4476</ul>

4477</div>

4478<section id="_TrialSearch_165_sid_16">

4480Current Clinical Trials</h4>

4483advanced clinical trial search</a>

4484 to find NCI-supported cancer clinical trials that are now enrolling patients. The search can be narrowed by location of the trial, type of treatment, name of the drug, and other criteria. <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/">

4485General information</a>

4486 about clinical trials is also available.</p>

4646

4647 <section id="_322">

4648

4649 <h2>

4650Treatment of Childhood High-Grade Astrocytomas</h2>

4651

4652 <div id="_section_322" class="pdq-sections">

4653<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

4654<h6>

4655In This Section</h6>

4656<ul>

4657<li>

4658<a href="#_324_toc">

4659Surgery</a>

4660</li>

4661<li>

4662<a href="#_326_toc">

4663Adjuvant Therapy<ul>

4664<li>

4665<a href="#_327_toc">

4666Radiation therapy</a>

4667</li>

4668<li>

4669<a href="#_329_toc">

4670Chemotherapy</a>

4671</li>

4672</ul>

4673</a>

4674</li>

4675<li>

4676<a href="#_392_toc">

4677Treatment Options Under Clinical Evaluation<ul>

4678<li>

4679<a href="#_TrialSearch_392_sid_9_toc">

4680Current Clinical Trials</a>

4681</li>

4682</ul>

4683</a>

4684</li>

4685</ul>

4686</nav>

4687<p id="_103" tabindex="-1">

4688To determine and implement optimal management, treatment of childhood high-grade astrocytomas should be guided by a multidisciplinary team of cancer specialists who have experience treating childhood brain tumors. </p>

4689<p id="_323" tabindex="-1">

4690Outcomes in high-grade gliomas occurring in childhood are often more favorable than that in adults. It is not clear whether this difference is caused by biologic variations in tumor characteristics, therapies used, tumor resectability, or other factors. </p>

4691<p id="_104" tabindex="-1">

4692The therapy for both children and adults with supratentorial high-grade astrocytoma includes surgery, radiation therapy, and chemotherapy. </p>

4693<p id="_369" tabindex="-1">

4694Standard treatment options for newly diagnosed childhood high-grade astrocytomas include the following:</p>

4695<div class="pdq-content-list">

4696<ol id="_386">

4697<li>

4698<a href="/types/brain/hp/child-astrocytoma-treament-pdq#_324">

4699Surgery</a>

4700.</li>

4701<li>

4702<a href="/types/brain/hp/child-astrocytoma-treament-pdq#_326">

4703Adjuvant therapy</a>

4704.<div class="pdq-content-list">

4705<ul id="_371">

4706<li>

4707<a href="/types/brain/hp/child-astrocytoma-treament-pdq#_327">

4708Radiation therapy</a>

4709.</li>

4710<li>

4711<a href="/types/brain/hp/child-astrocytoma-treament-pdq#_329">

4712Chemotherapy</a>

4713.</li>

4714</ul>

4715</div>

4716</li>

4717</ol>

4718</div>

4719<section id="_324">

4720<h3 id="_324_toc">

4721Surgery</h3>

4722<p id="_325" tabindex="-1">

4723The ability to obtain a complete resection is associated with a better prognosis.[<a href="#cit/section_6.1">

47241</a>

4725,<a href="#cit/section_6.2">

47262</a>

4727] Among patients treated with surgery, radiation therapy, and nitrosourea (lomustine)-based chemotherapy, the 5-year progression-free survival rate was 19% (&#177; 3%); the survival rate was 40% in those who had total resections.[<a href="#cit/section_6.3">

47283</a>

4729] Similarly, in a trial of multiagent chemoradiation therapy and adjuvant chemotherapy in addition to valproic acid, the overall 5-year event-free survival (EFS) rate was 13%, but for children with a complete resection of their tumor, the EFS rate was 48%.[<a href="#cit/section_6.4">

47304</a>

4731][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4732Level of evidence B4</a>

4733] </p>

4734</section>

4735<section id="_326">

4736<h3 id="_326_toc">

4737Adjuvant Therapy</h3>

4738<section id="_327">

4739<h4 id="_327_toc">

4740Radiation therapy</h4>

4741<p id="_328" tabindex="-1">

4742Radiation therapy is routinely administered to a field that widely encompasses the entire tumor. The radiation therapy dose to the tumor bed is usually at least 54 Gy. Despite such therapy, overall survival (OS) rates remain poor. Similarly poor survival is seen in children with spinal cord primaries and children with thalamic high-grade gliomas (i.e., diffuse midline gliomas, H3 K27M-mutant tumors) treated with radiation therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_6.5">

47435</a>

4744,<a href="#cit/section_6.6">

47456</a>

4746]; [<a href="#cit/section_6.7">

47477</a>

4748,<a href="#cit/section_6.8">

47498</a>

4750][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4751Level of evidence C1</a>

4752] </p>

4753</section>

4754<section id="_329">

4755<h4 id="_329_toc">

4756Chemotherapy</h4>

4757<p id="_330" tabindex="-1">

4758In one trial, children with glioblastoma who were treated on a prospective randomized trial with adjuvant lomustine, vincristine, and prednisone fared better than children treated with radiation therapy alone.[<a href="#cit/section_6.9">

47599</a>

4760] Furthermore, children who received lomustine in addition to temozolomide for subtotally resected tumors, especially glioblastoma with methylated O-6-methylguanine-DNA-methyltransferase (MGMT) overexpression, had a slightly improved outcome.[<a href="#cit/section_6.10">

476110</a>

4762] Patients with <em class="gene-name">

4763IDH1</em>

4764 mutations had an improved 1-year OS rate (100%) when compared with <em class="gene-name">

4765IDH1</em>

4766&#8211;wild-type tumors (1-year OS rate, 81%), highlighting the potential importance of underlying biological characteristics.[<a href="#cit/section_6.11">

476711</a>

4768]</p>

4769<p id="_331" tabindex="-1">

4770The use of temozolomide to treat glioblastoma was initially investigated in adults. In this population, the addition of temozolomide during and after radiation therapy resulted in improved 2-year EFS compared with treatment with radiation therapy alone. Adult patients with glioblastoma with an MGMT promoter benefitted from temozolomide, whereas those who did not have a methylated MGMT promoter did not.[<a href="#cit/section_6.12">

477112</a>

4772,<a href="#cit/section_6.13">

477313</a>

4774] The role of temozolomide given concurrently with radiation therapy for children with supratentorial high-grade glioma appears comparable to the outcome seen in children treated with nitrosourea-based therapy [<a href="#cit/section_6.14">

477514</a>

4776] and again demonstrated an EFS advantage for those children without MGMT overexpression. </p>

4777<p id="_1873" tabindex="-1">

4778The use of adjuvant bevacizumab after radiation therapy did not prolong OS or progression-free survival in pediatric patients with newly diagnosed high-grade gliomas.[<a href="#cit/section_6.15">

477915</a>

4780]</p>

4781<p id="_332" tabindex="-1">

4782Younger children may benefit from chemotherapy or consolidation with high-dose chemotherapy to delay, modify, or, in selected cases, obviate the need for radiation therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_6.16">

478316</a>

4784-<a href="#cit/section_6.18">

478518</a>

4786] </p>

4787</section>

4788</section>

4789<section id="_392">

4790<h3 id="_392_toc">

4791Treatment Options Under Clinical Evaluation</h3>

4792<p id="_393" tabindex="-1">

4793Early-phase therapeutic trials may be available for selected patients. These trials may be available via the <a href="https://www.childrensoncologygroup.org/" title="https://www.childrensoncologygroup.org/">

4794Children's Oncology Group (COG)</a>

4795, the <a href="http://www.pbtc.org/" title="http://www.pbtc.org/">

4796Pediatric Brain Tumor Consortium</a>

4797, or other entities. Information about National Cancer Institute (NCI)&#8211;supported clinical trials can be found on the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search">

4798NCI website</a>

4799. For information about clinical trials sponsored by other organizations, refer to the <a href="https://clinicaltrials.gov/" title="https://clinicaltrials.gov/">

4800ClinicalTrials.gov website</a>

4801.</p>

4802<p id="_1839" tabindex="-1">

4803The following is an example of a national and/or institutional clinical trial that is currently being conducted:</p>

4804<div class="pdq-content-list">

4805<ul id="_1840">

4806<li>

4807<strong>

4808<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT03919071">

4809ACNS1723 (NCT03919071)</a>

4810</strong>

4811 (Dabrafenib Combined With Trametinib After Radiation Therapy in Treating Patients With Newly-Diagnosed High-Grade Glioma)<strong>

4812:</strong>

4813 This phase II trial investigates the use of the combination of dabrafenib and trametinib after radiation therapy in children and young adults with high-grade gliomas who have a <em class="gene-name">

4814BRAF</em>

4815 V600 mutation. </li>

4816</ul>

4817</div>

4818<section id="_TrialSearch_392_sid_9">

4819<h4 id="_TrialSearch_392_sid_9_toc">

4820Current Clinical Trials</h4>

4821<p id="_TrialSearch_392_22" tabindex="-1">

4822Use our <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search/advanced" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search/advanced">

4823advanced clinical trial search</a>

4824 to find NCI-supported cancer clinical trials that are now enrolling patients. The search can be narrowed by location of the trial, type of treatment, name of the drug, and other criteria. <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/">

4825General information</a>

4826 about clinical trials is also available.</p>

4827</section>
